ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-item {
    counter-increment: list-counter-alpha;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-alpha, lower-alpha);
}

/* Level 3: Lowercase roman numerals */

    counter-reset: list-counter-roman;
}

    counter-increment: list-counter-roman;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-roman, lower-roman);
}

Management.pdf</p></div></div></div><div class="stElementContainer element-container st-emotion-cache-kj6hex eu6p4el1" data-testid="stElementContainer" data-stale="false" width="auto"><div class="stMarkdown" data-testid="stMarkdown"><div data-testid="stMarkdownContainer" class="st-emotion-cache-1104ytp e1icttdg0"><p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction">Introduction:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Traumatology 2 majorly deals with specific fractures and their management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-features-of-fractures-in-children">SPECIAL FEATURES OF FRACTURES IN CHILDREN:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-features-of-fractures-in-children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="learning-objectives">LEARNING OBJECTIVES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Classify growth plate injuries in children</li>
<li>Explain why greenstick injuries commonly occur in children</li>
<li>Discuss the rate of healing of fractures in children</li>
<li>State the possible effects of injuries on growth, in children</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="injuries-involving-growth-plate">Injuries involving growth plate:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#injuries-involving-growth-plate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Also known as epiphysial injuries</li>
<li>Each end of the long bones has a cartilaginous growth plate.</li>
<li>Most growth occurs away from the elbow and towards the knee.</li>
<li>The growth plate is a potentially weak point in the bone and is commonly injured in children.</li>
<li>Epiphysial injuries can be classified radiologically into five types as described by Salter and Harris.</li>
<li>Designated as Salter-Harris classification.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="salter-harris-classification">Salter-Harris classification:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#salter-harris-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Type I injury: complete separation of epiphysis at the growth plate without damage to the metaphysis or epiphysis. (Fracture involving the physis not extending to the metaphysis or epiphysis)</li>
<li>Type II injury: the most common type, with a characteristic triangular fragment of the metaphysis attached to the displaced epiphysis. (Fracture involving the physis extending to the metaphysis)</li>
<li>Type III injury: involves the articular surface with separation of an epiphysial fragment. (Fracture involving the physis extending to the epiphysis)</li>
<li>Type IV injury: fracture of the articular surface with extension across the growth plate into the metaphysis. (Fracture involving the physis extending to both metaphysis and epiphysis)</li>
<li>Type V injury: compression fracture involving part or all of the growth plate.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-d3f5cc69.jpeg" alt="img-0.jpeg">
Normal</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-9af8b795.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg">
Type 1 - 5%</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b26842e0.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg">
Type 2 - 75%</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-81498493.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Type 3 - 10%</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-e852ddeb.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Type 4 - 10%</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-6cf0052d.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
Type 5 - uncommon</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5361d6f0.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-c7496fc0.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-09e87706.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg"></p>
<p>Bone resilience:</p>
<p><a href="http://Www.cORTE.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Www.cORTE.com</a></p>
<p>Bones in children are more resilient and springier, withstanding greater deflection without fracture.</p>
<p>This explains the predominance of incomplete fractures of the greenstick type in children.</p>
<p>Periosteum</p>
<p>The periosteum in children's bones is attached only loosely to the diaphysis and is therefore easily stripped from the bone over a considerable part of its length by blood collecting beneath it.</p>
<p>This leads to abundance of callus following injury, even with little displacement of the fragments.</p>
<p>Sites of fracture:</p>
<p>Certain fractures that are common in adults are uncommon in children:</p>
<p>Fractures of:</p>
<ul>
<li>Scaphoid bone</li>
<li>Neck of femur</li>
<li>Trochanteric region of femur</li>
</ul>
<p>Some fractures are quite common in childhood:</p>
<ul>
<li>Supracondylar fractures of humerus</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Fractures of the capitulum of the humerus</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="healing">Healing:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#healing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Healing of childhood fractures is usually rapid, the younger the child the more rapid the healing.</p>
<p>In infancy a fracture may be soundly united in 2 or 3 weeks</p>
<p>In later childhood the average time required for union gradually increases.</p>
<p>Remodeling is very active and complete in early childhood; so much so that all evidence of a past fracture may be obliterated within a matter of months.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="effects-on-growth">Effects on growth:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#effects-on-growth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>After a fracture of a long bone in a child, growth is often accelerated for a time, perhaps from hyperemia of the neighboring epiphysial cartilage.</p>
<p>Growth may be seriously disturbed if the growth plate is damaged.</p>
<p>If the whole area of the growth plate is fused, all growth ceases at that site.</p>
<p>The degree of consequent shortening will depend on the age at which premature fusion occurred; the younger the patient at the time of fusion, the greater the eventual shortening.</p>
<p>If premature fusion occurs in only a part of the epiphysial plate, further growth will be prevented at that point but will continue in the undamaged part of the plate, leading to angulation deformity.</p>
<p>Angulation will also occur if there is premature arrest in one bone of a pair, as in the forearm or leg.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-the-shoulder-girdle-and-the-upper-limb">FRACTURES OF THE SHOULDER GIRDLE AND THE UPPER LIMB.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-the-shoulder-girdle-and-the-upper-limb" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It comprises the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Clavicle</li>
<li>Scapula</li>
<li>Shoulder Joint</li>
<li>Humerus</li>
<li>Elbow Joint</li>
<li>Forearm Bones-radius/ulna</li>
<li>Wrist Joint</li>
<li>Carpals (Scaphoid Bone)</li>
<li>Metacarpals(5) and phalanges(14)</li>
</ul>
<p>Mechanism of injury for upper limb fractures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Mostly Indirect</li>
<li>Commonly described as a fall on outstretched hand</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>Type of injury depends on position of the upper limb at the time of impact: Flexed, Extended, adducted, abducted, pronated or supinated</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-of-the-clavicle">FRACTURE OF THE CLAVICLE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-of-the-clavicle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Clavicle Shaft Fractures are common pediatric fractures that most commonly occur due to a fall on an outstretched arm or direct trauma to lateral aspect of shoulder.</p>
<p>Diagnosis can be made with plain radiographs.</p>
<p>Treatment is generally nonoperative management with a sling. Surgical management is indicated for open fractures or those associated with impending soft tissue compromise.</p>
<ul>
<li>Commonest site is the middle one third (mid-shaft) of the clavicle, at about the junction of the middle and lateral thirds of the clavicle.</li>
<li>Some fractures also occur at the outer end of the clavicle.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury">Mechanism of injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Mainly due to indirect injury</li>
<li>A fall on the shoulder with a direct blow to the tip of the shoulder</li>
<li>A fall onto the outstretched hand</li>
<li>Direct injury often leads to comminuted fracture.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="displacement">Displacement:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#displacement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In the common mid-shaft fracture, the lateral fragment is displaced downwards inferomedially by pectoralis major and the weight of the arm.</li>
<li>Medial fragments pulled posterosuperiorly by sternocleidomastoid.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical features:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The arm is clasped to the chest to prevent movement</li>
<li>There is an obvious subcutaneous lump at the site of the clavicle</li>
<li>Occasionally, a sharp fragment threatens the skin</li>
<li>Pain and occasionally ecchymosis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="presentation">PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>History</li>
<li>popping or cracking sound near shoulder after fall</li>
<li>Symptoms</li>
<li>acute onset of anterior shoulder pain or directly over clavicle</li>
<li>Physical exam</li>
<li>inspection</li>
<li>tender, swelling, crepitus and deformity over clavicle</li>
<li>skin tenting (impending open fracture)</li>
<li>neurovascular exam</li>
<li>assess subclavian vessels and brachial plexus</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Associated conditions:</p>
<p>Medical conditions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hemo/Pneumothorax</li>
<li>Closed head injury</li>
</ul>
<p>Orthopaedics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ipsilateral scapular fracture</li>
<li>Scapulothoracic dislocation</li>
<li>Rib fractures</li>
<li>Neurovascular injury</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-97d744cd.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5cb3068e.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg"></p>
<p>X-ray findings:</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>The fracture is usually in the middle third of the bone</li>
<li>The outer fragment lies below the inner fragment, and is usually medially displaced.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-57f81b5a.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<p>oNondisplaced o&lt; 100% displacement oNonoperative o</p>
<p>oDisplaced o&gt; 100% displacement oOperative o</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-a42bc4e6.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-c8d142e2.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Conservative treatment</li>
<li>Support the arm in an arm sling for 2 weeks. This provides good pain relief.</li>
<li>As soon as the initial sharp pain subsides, active shoulder exercises should be begun to restore full mobility</li>
<li>Fractures of the clavicle unite readily.</li>
<li>The only common residual disability is a palpable or visible irregularity of bone at the site of the fracture.</li>
<li>In children normal contour is quickly restored by remodeling.</li>
<li>In adults some thickening may remain.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nonoperative">Nonoperative<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nonoperative" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="osling-immobilization">osling immobilization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osling-immobilization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p><strong>indications</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>&lt; 2cm shortening and displacement</li>
<li>&lt; 1cm displacement of the superior shoulder suspensory complex</li>
<li>closed and no neurovascular injury</li>
<li>low demand patient</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>techniques</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>sling</li>
<li>figure-of-8 strap</li>
<li>elevate and extend shoulder to bring distal fragment to the proximal fragment</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>outcomes</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>figure-of-8 associated with more pain, shortening, and lower compliance than sling</li>
<li>no difference in functional or cosmetic outcomes between sling and figure-of-eight braces</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="operative">Operative<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#operative" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>open reduction internal fixation (ORIF)
indications</p>
<p>absolute
open fractures
displaced fracture with skin tenting
subclavian artery or vein injury
floating shoulder (clavicle and scapular neck fracture)
relative and controversial indications
displaced with &gt;2cm shortening</p>
<p>bilateral displaced clavicle fractures
brachial plexus injury (questionable because 66% have spontaneous return)
closed head injury
seizure disorder
polytrauma patient</p>
<p>techniques
intramedullary fixation
open reduction internal fixation with plate and screws
outcomes
operative fixation has higher union rate (&gt;94%)</p>
<p>similar or better functional outcomes than nonoperative
faster time to union - operative (16.4 weeks) vs. non-operative (28.4 weeks)</p>
<p>Complications:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Early: very rarely: -</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pneumothorax</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Damage to subclavian vessels</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Injury to brachial plexus</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Late:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Non-union</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Malunion  common. Leaves a lump at the site</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Stiffness of the shoulder</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Poor cosmesis</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>COMPLICATIONS OF OPERATIVE MANAGEMENT:</p>
<ul>
<li>Hardware irritation</li>
<li>Infection</li>
<li>Neurovascular injury</li>
<li>Hardware failure</li>
</ul>
<p>FRACTURES OF THE SCAPULA.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>scapula Fractures are uncommon fractures to the shoulder girdle caused by high energy trauma and associated with pulmonary injury, head injury, and increased injury severity scores.</p>
<p>Diagnosis can be made with plain radiographs and CT studies are helpful for fracture characterization and surgical planning.</p>
<p>Treatment is usually nonoperative with a sling. Surgical management is indicated for intra-articular fractures, displaced scapular body/neck fractures, open fractures, and those associated with glenohumeral instability.</p>
<ul>
<li>The scapula may be fractured in the:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Body</li>
<li>Neck</li>
<li>Acromion process</li>
<li>Coracoid process</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>The fractures are not very common</li>
<li>The mechanism of injury is by direct violence.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-15c13d35.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-325e763e.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Fracture of the body of the scapula:</p>
<ul>
<li>May be comminuted, but there is no important displacement because the fragments are held in position by muscles that attach both on the costal and outer surfaces.</li>
</ul>
<p>mechanism of injury</p>
<p><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\circ</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4445em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span> high-energy trauma (80-90%)</p>
<p>motor vehicle collisions</p>
<p>account for <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex-error" title="ParseError: KaTeX parse error: Expected 'EOF', got '&amp;' at position 1: &amp;gt;70\%" style="color: rgb(204, 0, 0);">&amp;gt;70\%</span></span> of scapula fractures</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>oindirect trauma through fall on outstretched hand</p>
<p>oglenohumeral dislocation</p>
<p>anterior dislocation leads to anterior rim fracture</p>
<p>posterior dislocation leads to posterior rim fracture</p>
<p>seizure</p>
<p>electric shock</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-of-the-neck-of-the-scapula">FRACTURE OF THE NECK OF THE SCAPULA:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-of-the-neck-of-the-scapula" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>the fracture extends from the scapular notch to the axillary border of the scapula, so that the part bearing the articular surface is detached in one piece from the body of the bone.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>the glenoid fragment may be displaced downwards.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-of-the-acromion-process">FRACTURE OF THE ACROMION PROCESS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-of-the-acromion-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>the fracture occurs at a variable distance from the tip of the acromion.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>the fracture may be a crack without displacement, or the acromion may be comminuted and displaced downwards.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-of-the-coracoid-process">FRACTURE OF THE CORACOID PROCESS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-of-the-coracoid-process" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>the fracture may be a crack without displacement, or there may be a complete fracture with separation and downward displacement of the coracoid process.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Severe pain</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The arm is held immobile</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>There may be extravasation of blood into the tissues, with widespread ecchymosis around the scapular region.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Attention should be given to restoration of shoulder function.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Support the arm in a sling to relieve pain and provide comfort. The sling can be worn for up to 6 weeks</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>As soon as pain begins to subside, active shoulder exercises are begun, and continued until full range of movement is regained.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>For badly comminuted or severely displaced fracture of the acromion, operation to excise the acromion is advised.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-726adb09.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>complications</p>
<ul>
<li>oneurovascular injury</li>
<li>omalunion</li>
<li>hardware failure</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Post-traumatic glenohumeral arthritis</li>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>intra-articular glenoid fracture with residual step-off/displacement</li>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>conservative management</li>
<li>NSAIDs, therapy, injections</li>
<li>shoulder arthroplasty (total vs. reverse)</li>
<li>Malunion</li>
<li>Recurrent glenohumeral instability</li>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>younger patients</li>
<li>larger degree of bone loss (anterior or posterior)</li>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>bony fixation (open or percutaneous)</li>
<li>arthroscopic vs. open suture anchor repair with labral advancement</li>
<li>more useful for smaller bony fragments which are not able to be fixated otherwise</li>
<li>Neurovascular injury</li>
<li>musculocutaneous n.</li>
<li>axillary n.</li>
<li>suprascapular n.</li>
<li>circumflex scapular v.</li>
<li>posterior humeral circumflex v.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dislocation-of-the-shoulder-joint">DISLOCATION OF THE SHOULDER JOINT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dislocation-of-the-shoulder-joint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Among the large joints, the shoulder is the one that most commonly dislocates. Factors contributing include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Shallowness of the glenoid socket.</li>
<li>Extra ordinary range of movements.</li>
<li>Ligaments laxity</li>
<li>Glenoid dysplasia</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Vulnerability of the joint during stressful activities of the upper limb.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="anterior-dislocation">ANTERIOR DISLOCATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anterior-dislocation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Mechanism of injury:</p>
<p>An anterior shoulder dislocation is usually caused by a blow to the abducted, externally rotated, and extended arm (e.g., player blocking a basketball shot). Alternatively, a blow to the posterior humerus or a fall on an outstretched arm may cause an anterior dislocation.</p>
<p>A fall on the hand. The head of the humerus is driven forward, tearing the capsule and producing avulsion of the glenoid labrum. (The Bankart lesion). Occasionary the posterior lateral part of the head is crushed. Nearly always the arm drops, bringing the head to its Sub coracoid position.</p>
<p>Clinical features.</p>
<ol>
<li>Severe pain</li>
<li>Patient supports the arm with the opposite hand.</li>
<li>Flattened lateral outline of the shoulder.</li>
<li>Bulge felt below the clavicle.</li>
</ol>
<p>NOTE.</p>
<p>The arm must always be examined for nerve and vessel injury before reduction is attempted.</p>
<p>INVESTIGATIONS.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>&gt;History</p>
<p>&gt;Clinical examination</p>
<p>&gt;X-ray Anterior posterior and Lateral views confirms the diagnosis.</p>
<p>TREATMENT.</p>
<p>Reduction is always done under General Anesthesia&gt;GA.</p>
<p>TECHNIQUES:</p>
<ol>
<li>Stimson's technique&gt;The patient left prone with the arm hanging over the side of the bed. After 15-20 minutes the shoulder may reduce.</li>
<li>Hippocratic method&gt;Gentle traction applied to the arm with the shoulder in slight abduction, while an assistant applies firm counter traction to the body (a towel slung around the patient's chest under the axilla is helpful). COMMONLY USED.</li>
<li>Kocher's method &gt;Elbow bent 90 degrees and held close to the body, no traction should be applied. The arm is slowly rotated 75 degrees laterally, the point of the elbow is lifted forwards and finally the arm is rotated medially. This technique carries the risk of nerve, vessel and bone injury and is not recommended.</li>
<li>The patient sitting on a reduction chair and with gentle traction of the arm over the back of the padded chair the dislocation is reduced.</li>
</ol>
<p>Do a check X-ray to confirm reduction and exclude any fracture?</p>
<p>Arm sling applied for 1 to 3 weeks.</p>
<p>Analgesics.</p>
<p>Operative management:</p>
<p>-Unreduced dislocations
-Fracture dislocations</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reccurent dislocations
-High demand athletes.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Early.</p>
<p>Rotator curve tear</p>
<p>Nerve injury&gt;Axillary---supplies the deltoid muscle.</p>
<p>Others, Radial, Musculocutaneous, Median or Ulna nerves.</p>
<p>Vascular artery&gt; Axillary artery.</p>
<p>Fracture dislocation&gt;Fracture proximal humerus can occur. In this case open reduction is required to avoid later subacromial impingement.</p>
<p>Late.</p>
<p>Shoulder stiffness. Common in patients over 40 years of age.</p>
<p>Unreduced dislocation&gt;May be undiagnosed---In unconscious or old patients. (6weeks injury attempt closed reduction). More than 6weeks on the young operative reduction is done.</p>
<p>Recurrent dislocation&gt;Arthroscopic operation is done.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-093bcd1e.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-4312bb50.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>POSTERIOR DISLOCATION OF THE SHOULDER.</p>
<p>It is rare accounts for less than 2% of all the dislocations.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury">Mechanism of injury.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Very severe indirect force&gt;Fit or Convulsion or Electric shock.</p>
<p>A fall on the flexed, adducted arm or on the outstretched hand.</p>
<p>Direct blow to the front of the joint.</p>
<p>Clinical features.</p>
<ol>
<li>Arm is held in internal rotation.</li>
<li>Front of the shoulder looks flat with a prominent coracoid.</li>
<li>Pain.</li>
<li>May be missed. (Frequently).</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="investigations">INVESTIGATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>X-ray Ap and Lateral views.</p>
<p>CT SCAN.</p>
<p>MRI</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patient under general anaesthesia(GA). The arm is pulled with the shoulder in adduction, rotate arm gently laterally while the humeral head is pushed forwards.</p>
<p>Arm sling 3-6 weeks</p>
<p>Physiotherapy</p>
<p>Analgesics</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Un-reduced dislocation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Open reduction indicated.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Recurrent dislocation or subluxation.</li>
</ol>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-3ce24eaf.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="inferior-dislocation-of-the">INFERIOR DISLOCATION OF THE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#inferior-dislocation-of-the" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="shoulder-luxatio-erecta">SHOULDER [LUXATIO ERECTA].<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shoulder-luxatio-erecta" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Rare but it demands early recognition because the</p>
<p>consequences are potentially very serious. The humeral head is levered out of its socket and pokes into the axilla. The arm remains fixed in abduction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mechanism-of-injury">Mechanism of injury.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A severe hyper abducted force causes the injury. Soft tissue injury may be severe and includes avulsion of the capsule and surrounding tendons, rupture of muscles fracture of the glenoid. Proximal humerus and damage to the brachial plexus and axillary artery can occur.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5cb751bb.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pain.</p>
<p>Arm locked in almost full abduction (confirms the diagnosis).</p>
<p>Humeral head may be felt in or below the axilla.</p>
<p>Always examine for neurovascular damage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="investigations">INVESTIGATIONS.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ap/X-ray shows the dislocation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patient under GA.</p>
<p>Pull upwards in the line of the abducted arm, counter traction downwards over the top of the shoulder.</p>
<p>Open reduction.</p>
<p>Arm sling for 3 weeks.</p>
<p>Analgesics</p>
<p>Examine again after reduction for evidence of neurovascular injury.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shoulder-dislocation-in-children">SHOULDER DISLOCATION IN CHILDREN.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shoulder-dislocation-in-children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Traumatic dislocation of the shoulder is exceedingly rare in children. The shoulder slips out unexpectedly during everyday activities' X-ray may confirm the diagnosis.</p>
<p>Most of these children have generalized joint laxity and some have glenoid dysplasia or muscle patterning disorders.</p>
<p>19</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>a. Physiotherapy.
b. Reconstructive surgery.</p>
<p>Reduction maneuver:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-f0866f63.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>Can occur in the proximal humerus, shaft of humerus and distal humerus.</li>
</ul>
<p>Classification:</p>
<ul>
<li>#s of humerus may be classified into six groups:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>Fractures of the neck of humerus</li>
<li>Fracture of the greater and lesser tuberosity</li>
<li>Fracture of the shaft</li>
<li>Supracondylar fracture</li>
<li>Fracture of the condyles</li>
<li>Fracture of the epicondyles.</li>
</ol>
<p>Fracture neck humerus</p>
<ul>
<li>Occurs most often in elderly women, in whom it is a common injury.</li>
<li>In most of such patients there is some degree of rarefaction from osteoporosis, so that the bones are relatively weak.</li>
<li>Results in fragility fracture.</li>
<li>Follows a fall onto the limb.</li>
</ul>
<p>Features:</p>
<ul>
<li>Displacement is variable</li>
<li>May be no displacement</li>
<li>There may be moderate or severe tilting of the head fragment so that the shaft appears either abducted or adducted in relation to it.</li>
<li>In well over half the cases the fragments are firmly impacted together so that the bone moves as one piece.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinically">Clinically:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinically" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The fracture may easily be overlooked if it is impacted.</li>
<li>May be able to use the limb to some extent without severe pain.</li>
<li>The possibility of a fracture should always be suspected from the nature of the injury, especially if the patient is an elderly woman.</li>
<li>Extensive bruising in the upper and middle parts of the upper arm also lend support to the diagnosis.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="radiographs">Radiographs:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radiographs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Show the fracture</li>
<li>Do not indicate with certainty whether the fracture is impacted or not</li>
<li>This can best be ascertained by clinical examination.</li>
<li>Severe pain on slight movement of the arm implies the fracture is not impacted.</li>
<li>Passive movement of the limb through a reasonable range without severe pain implies the fracture is impacted.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Impacted fractures: -</li>
<li>Immobilization is unnecessary</li>
<li>Support in an arm sling for 4-6 weeks</li>
<li>Active and assisted shoulder movements are begun as soon as pain subsides.</li>
<li>Displaced fractures: -</li>
<li>Are best managed by open reduction and internal fixation by use of metal plates and screws.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">Complications:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Joint stiffness</li>
<li>Shoulder is prone to stiffness in elderly persons.</li>
<li>Arterial injury</li>
<li>Brachial artery</li>
<li>Nerve injury</li>
<li>Axillary nerve.</li>
<li>Treatment is expectant.</li>
<li>Dislocation of the shoulder</li>
<li>Rarely the fracture is associated with dislocation of the shoulder.</li>
<li>The dislocation should be reduced first.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="impacted-and-displaced-neck-humerus">IMPACTED AND DISPLACED # NECK HUMERUS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impacted-and-displaced-neck-humerus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-d3cccb5e.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="plating-and-rush-nails">PLATING AND RUSH NAILS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#plating-and-rush-nails" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-81bb76af.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5882a361.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms">MECHANISMS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Usually caused by a fall onto the shoulder</li>
<li>Occurs in adults of any age, but mainly the elderly.</li>
<li>Usually, no marked displacement</li>
<li>The tuberosity may be comminuted</li>
<li>At times avulsion of a fragment occurs by the action of attached muscles.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Undisplaced fracture:</li>
<li>Requires no rigid splintage.</li>
<li>u-slab application + Pouch armsling</li>
<li>Shoulder exercises to restore movement and function is all that is needed.</li>
<li>Avulsed widely separated fragment:</li>
<li>Is held by a screw through open reduction and internal fixation.</li>
<li>Shoulder exercises should be practiced thereafter.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">Complications:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Painful arc syndrome (supraspinatus syndrome)</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>-Thickening or irregularity of the greater tuberosity may interfere with the abduction at the glenohumeral joint, because the thickened area may impinge against the acromion process or coraco-acromial ligament, causing pain.</p>
<p>-Occurs mainly during the middle phase of abduction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-shaft-humerus">FRACTURE SHAFT HUMERUS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-shaft-humerus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Commonest site is in the middle third</li>
</ul>
<p>Humeral shaft fractures are common fractures of the diaphysis of the humerus, which may be associated with radial nerve injury.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made with orthogonal radiographs of the humerus.</p>
<p>Treatment can be nonoperative or operative depending on location of fracture, fracture morphology, and association with other ipsilateral injuries.</p>
<p>Mechanism of injury:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Indirect twisting force</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Indirect injury results in Spiral fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A fall on the elbow with the arm abducted may cause an oblique or transverse fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Direct blows result in transverse, short oblique or comminuted fracture</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>OTA</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>bone number: 1</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture location: 2</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture pattern: simple: A, wedge: B, complex: C</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Descriptive</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture location: proximal, middle or distal third</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture pattern: spiral, transverse, comminuted</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Holstein-Lewis fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>a spiral fracture of the distal one-third of the humeral shaft commonly associated with neuropraxia of the radial nerve (22% incidence)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Clinical features</p>
<ul>
<li>Painful arm</li>
<li>Extensive bruising</li>
<li>Swelling</li>
<li>Loss of function</li>
<li>May present with radial nerve injury</li>
</ul>
<p>Radiological findings</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>Will show:</li>
<li>Site of fracture</li>
<li>Fracture pattern</li>
<li>Any displacement</li>
<li>May show features of pathological fracture.
Proximal half of the humerus is a common site for pathological fracture from carcinomatous metastases.</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment:</p>
<ul>
<li>Fractures of the humerus heal readily</li>
<li>They do not require perfect reduction or immobilization</li>
<li>The weight of the arm with an external cast is usually enough to pull the fragments into alignment.</li>
<li>A U-slab plaster is applied to maintain alignment for 6-8 weeks.</li>
<li>Further support is provided by an arm sling.</li>
<li>Active shoulder exercises are encouraged, pendulum exercises within a week, but active abduction only when the fracture is sticky  after 6 weeks.</li>
<li>Once the # is sticky, a well molded cast brace may be used.</li>
</ul>
<p>Alternative methods of treatment:</p>
<ul>
<li>When the fragments are very unstable, in case of gross displacement, or in case of pathological fracture from a metastatic tumor, open reduction and internal fixation is done.</li>
<li>Plate and screws</li>
<li>Intramedullary nail (with locking screws)</li>
<li>In the case of contaminated open fracture, or an infected fracture, external fixation is done.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-746c280a.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-4929f6de.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Nonoperative</strong></p>
<p>immobilization (coaptation splint followed by a functional brace)</p>
<p><strong>indications</strong></p>
<p>indicated in vast majority of humeral shaft fractures criteria for acceptable alignment include:</p>
<ul>
<li>&lt; 20 anterior angulation</li>
<li>&lt; 30 varus/valgus angulation</li>
<li>&lt; 3 cm shortening</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>contraindications</strong></p>
<p><strong>absolute</strong></p>
<p>severe soft tissue injury or bone loss</p>
<p>vascular injury requiring repair</p>
<p>brachial plexus injury.</p>
<p><strong>Operative</strong></p>
<p><strong>external fixation (Exfix)</strong></p>
<p><strong>indications</strong></p>
<p>high energy complex or comminuted fracture</p>
<p>open fracture</p>
<p>significant soft tissue or bony defect</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stMarkdownPre" class="st-emotion-cache-acwcvw e1icttdg5"><div class="stCode st-emotion-cache-6ml83 e1xss9yb4" data-testid="stCode"><pre class="st-emotion-cache-1nqbjoj e1xss9yb2"><div style="background-color: transparent;"><code class="language-txt" style="white-space: pre;"><span>floating elbow

<p>Complications:</p>
<p>Early: _______________</p>
<ul>
<li>Nerve injury  radial nerve because of its close contact with the bone as it winds round its posterior aspect in the radial groove.</li>
<li>Nerve</li>
<li>radial nerve</li>
<li>courses along spiral groove</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>14cm proximal to the lateral epicondyle</li>
<li>20cm proximal to the medial epicondyle</li>
</ul>
<p>Vascular injury - brachial artery</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="late">Late:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#late" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Non-union</p>
<ul>
<li>Delayed union</li>
<li>Joint stiffness - shoulder
Mal union</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="radial-nerve-injury">Radial nerve injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radial-nerve-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Results in Wrist drop  due to paralysis of the extensor muscles of the wrist, fingers and thumb.</li>
<li>Brachioradialis and supinator muscles may also be paralyzed.</li>
<li>Sensory loss in a small area at the anatomical snuff box</li>
<li>Associated with fracture humerus in up to  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>12</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">12\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">12%</span></span></span></span></span>  of fractures [10-15%]</li>
<li>2/3 (8%) of Radial injury are Neuropraxia</li>
<li>1/3 (4%) are nerve lacerations or transection</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management-of-radial-nerve-injury">Management of radial nerve injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-radial-nerve-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>When present in open fractures - immediate exploration and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\pm</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord"></span></span></span></span></span>  repair</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In closed fractures it is assumed that the nerve is in continuity and spontaneous recovery is awaited (conservative treatment)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Initial management is doing Nerve Conduction Studies (NCS) and</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Electromyography (EMG) and awaiting for spontaneous recovery.</p>
<ul>
<li>Recovery usually starts after few days but may take up to 9 months for full recovery</li>
<li>If there is no sign of spontaneous recovery by 12 weeks, then exploration of the nerve should be carried out.</li>
</ul>
<p>u-slab/coaptation splint</p>
<p>functional brace</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-17e1c876.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="indications-for-orif">Indications for ORIF:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#indications-for-orif" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Very unstable fragments</li>
<li>Bilateral humeral fractures</li>
<li>Severe multiple injuries</li>
<li>Segmental fractures</li>
<li>Pathological fracture</li>
<li>Radial nerve palsy after manipulation</li>
<li>Unconscious patient</li>
<li>Bed-ridden patient</li>
<li>Delayed-Union, Non-Union and Mal-Union</li>
</ul>
<p>PLATING with screws</p>
<p>K-WIRE FIXATION</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5099d55b.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-9f51a009.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-condyles">FRACTURE CONDYLES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-condyles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Are relatively uncommon, but often troublesome.
They occur mainly in children.</p>
<p>MODE OF INJURY:</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>A fall on the point of the elbow drives the olecranon process upwards, splitng the condyles apart.</li>
<li>The associated soft tissue injury is usually severe.</li>
<li>The lateral condyle is fractured much more commonly than the medial.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Severe pain in the elbow region</li>
<li>Swelling of the elbow</li>
<li>Deformity - the elbow is wider than normal</li>
<li>The tip of the olecranon is higher than normal</li>
<li>There is tenderness over the lateral condyle</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="x-ray-findings">X-RAY FINDINGS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#x-ray-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The usual lateral condylar fracture extends obliquely upwards and laterally from the capitular surface.</li>
<li>In young children the greater part of the detached fragment may be cartilaginous, so that the fragment appears much smaller as seen radiographically than in fact.</li>
<li>Fracture could be T-shaped, Y-shaped or even comminuted.
The fractures involve the joint surface.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Undisplaced fracture:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Splinting of the arm in a back-slab with the elbow flexed at 90 degrees, with the forearm in neutral position and wrist extended.</li>
<li>The splint is removed after 3-4 weeks and exercises are encouraged.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Displaced fracture:</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Requires accurate reduction and internal fixation.</li>
<li>For a moderately displaced fragment, it may be possible to manipulate it into position by extending the elbow and pressing upon the condyle, then fixing the fragment with percutaneous pins.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>If this fails, open reduction and internal fixation with pins is done, same as for all separated fractures.</li>
<li>Immobilize the arm in a cast for 3-4 weeks, then remove cast and pins.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-union">Non-union:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-union" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Condylar fractures are prone to non-union, and may lead to deformity of the elbow and to osteoarthritis.</li>
<li>Treatment is to refresh the fracture surfaces then accurate reduction and fixation with a screw.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="deformity">Deformity:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#deformity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>May be caused by persistent upward displacement of the fractured condyle, or by retardation of epiphyseal growth on the affected side from damage to the growing epiphyseal cartilage.</li>
<li>If the lateral condyle is affected the deformity will be that of cubitus valgus, whereas involvement of the medial condyle causes cubitus varus.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frictional-neuritis-of-the-ulnar-nerve">Frictional neuritis of the ulnar nerve:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frictional-neuritis-of-the-ulnar-nerve" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In cases of cubitus valgus there is a risk of ulnar paralysis from frictional neuritis where the nerve is angled behind the medial epicondyle.</li>
<li>Treatment: the nerve should be transposed from its post-epicondylar groove to a new bed in front of the elbow.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="osteoarthritis">Osteoarthritis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteoarthritis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Is liable to occur when a condylar fracture leaves permanent deformity or irregularity of the articular surface.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stiffness-of-the-elbow-joint">Stiffness of the elbow joint:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stiffness-of-the-elbow-joint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Comminuted intercondylar fractures usually result in some degree of stiffness.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fracture-epicondyles">FRACTURE EPICONDYLES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-epicondyles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The medial epicondyle is fractured more commonly than the lateral.</p>
