<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mechanisms-of-injury">MECHANISMS OF INJURY:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>Occurs more often in children than in adults</li>
<li>It is often an avulsion injury, the epicondyle being pulled off by the attached flexor muscles (or medial ligament) during a fall on the out-stretched hand with the wrist and elbow extended, and the elbow wrenched into valgus.</li>
<li>If the elbow subluxate, the small fragment may be dragged into the joint.</li>
<li>The fracture may also be caused by direct violence</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pain, swelling and bruising on the medial side of the elbow following an injury.</li>
<li>Sensation in the fingers should be tested to exclude ulnar nerve damage.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="x-ray-findings">X-RAY FINDINGS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#x-ray-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="ap-view">AP view:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ap-view" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>The medial epicondylar epiphysis may be tilted or shifted downwards.</li>
<li>If the joint is dislocated the fragment lies distal to the lower humerus.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="lateral-view">Lateral view:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lateral-view" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>May show the epicondyle looking like a loose body in the joint.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Minor displacement may be disregarded.</li>
<li>The elbow should be immobilized in plaster for 3 weeks to relieve pain. A/E BACKSLAB</li>
<li>Thereafter joint exercises to restore movement.</li>
<li>If the epicondyle is trapped in the joint, it must be freed.</li>
<li>This can be done by:</li>
<li>Manipulation with the elbow in valgus and the wrist hyperextended to pull on the flexor muscles</li>
<li>Opening the joint and retrieving the fragment, fixing it back in position by sutures.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="early">Early:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#early" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Ulnar nerve damage is common due to immediate direct injury in displaced fractures.</li>
<li>Inclusion of the epicondylar fragment in the joint.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="late">Late:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#late" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>Ulnar nerve palsy from frictional neuritis due to roughened bony groove.</li>
<li>Stiffness of the elbow</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis</li>
<li>Malunion</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pediatric-supracondylar-humerus-fracture">Pediatric Supracondylar Humerus Fracture:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pediatric-supracondylar-humerus-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-1bd73157.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-f45284f6.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg">
Fig 1. Halluciformis of our 45 during a human at an open vessel, long the elbow cross, along the anterior aspect.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-d8c52c72.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg">
Fig 2. 45' and lateral radiographs showing paravertebralic displaced right triceps condylar humerus fracture.</p>
<p>Supracondylar Fractures are one of the most common traumatic fractures seen in children and most commonly occur in children 5-7 years of age from a fall on an outstretched hand.</p>
<p>Diagnosis can be made with plain radiographs.</p>
<p>Treatment is usually closed reduction and percutaneous pinning (CRPP), with the urgency depending on presence or absence of hand perfusion.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>They are among the commonest fractures in children. The distal fragment may be displaced either posteriorly or anteriorly.</p>
<p>Mechanism of injury.</p>
<p>Usually a fall on the outstretched hand.</p>
<p>95% of all cases causes posterior angulation or displacement [Hyperextension injury].</p>
<p>The humerus breaks just above the condyles. The distal fragment is pushed backwards and (because the forearm is usually in pronation) twisted inwards. The jagged end of the proximal fragments pokes into the soft tissues anteriorly, sometimes injuring the brachial artery or median nerve</p>
<p>Anterior displacement is rare; it is thought to be due to direct violence (e.g. a fall on the point of the elbow) with the joint in flexion.</p>
<p>Associated injuries</p>
<p>oneuropraxia</p>
<p>anterior interosseous nerve (AIN) neurapraxia (branch of median n.) the most common nerve palsy seen with supracondylar humerus fractures</p>
<p>radial nerve palsy</p>
<p>second most common neurapraxia (close second)</p>
<p>ulnar nerve palsy</p>
<p>seen with flexion-type injury patterns</p>
<p>nearly all cases of neurapraxia following supracondylar humerus fractures resolve spontaneously</p>
<p>ovascular compromise (5-17%)</p>
<p>rich collateral circulation can maintain circulation despite vascular injury</p>
<p>oipsilateral distal radius fractures</p>
<p>ANATOMY</p>
<p>Ossification centers of elbow</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>oage of ossification/appearance and age of fusion are two independent events that must be differentiated</p>
<p>e.g., internal (medial epicondyle) apophysis</p>
<p>ossifies/appears at age 6 years (table below)</p>
<p>fuses at age  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo></mo><mn>17</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\sim 17</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.3669em;"></span><span class="mrel"></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">17</span></span></span></span></span>  years (is the last to fuse)</p>
<p>+/- one year, varies between boys and girl</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>Ossification Centers of the Elbow</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Ossification Center</th><th>Years at ossification (appear on xray)</th><th>Years at fusion (appear on xray)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Capitellum 1 12</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Radial Head</td><td>4 15</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Medial epicondyle</td><td>6 17</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Trochlea 8 12</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Olecranon 10 15</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Lateral epicondyle</td><td>12 12</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="physical-exam">PHYSICAL EXAM:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-exam" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Pain
Refusal/inability to move the elbow</p>
<ul>
<li>Deformity proportional to displacement</li>
<li>Swelling &amp; bruising
Skin integrity</li>
<li>Tenting/compromise</li>
<li>Open fractures</li>
</ul>
<p>Brachialis sign:</p>
<ul>
<li>Antecubital ecchymosis
Skin puckering</li>
<li>Subcutaneous bone fragment (soft-tissue interposition)</li>
</ul>
<p>(Indicator of: )</p>
<p>Significant injury and swelling</p>
<ul>
<li>Potential failure of closed reduction</li>
</ul>
<p>Neurovascular exam:</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Relatively high rate of neurovascular injuries due to intimate relationship of nerves and artery to displaced fracture fragments</p>
<ul>
<li>Neurologic exam can be challenging in injured child but important to document pre-manipulation exam</li>
<li>Pulseless hand may still be perfused because of excellent collateral circulation in pediatric elbow</li>
</ul>
<p>Classification.</p>
<p>GARTLANDS CLASSIFICATION:</p>
<p>Type I---An undisplaced fracture.</p>
<p>Type II---An angulated fracture with posterior cortex still in continuity.</p>
<p>IIA---A less severe injury with the distal fragment merely angulated.</p>
<p>IIB---A severe injury, the fragment is both angulated and mal-rotated.</p>
<p>Type III---A completely displaced fracture. (Although the posterior periosteum is usually preserved, which will assist surgical reduction).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-4c97aa15.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg"></p>
<p>Feel the pulse and check the capillary return. Wrist and hand should be examined for evidence of nerve injury.</p>
<p>IMAGING":</p>
<p>Recommended views are:</p>
<p>Ap &amp; lateral views</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Measurement of Baumann's angle is useful in assessing the degree of medial angulation before and after reduction.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-9fc5713c.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If there is even a suspicion of a fracture the elbow is gently splinted in 30 degrees of flexion to prevent movement and possible neurovascular injury the x-ray examination.</p>
<p>Type I: Undisplaced #</p>
<p>Elbow is immobilized at 90 degrees and a neutral rotation in a light weight splint or cast and arm is supported by a sling. Check x-ray 5-7 days later to check if there is any displacement. Retain the splint for 3 weeks and supervised movement is allowed.</p>
<p>Type IIA: Posteriorly Angulated Fracture-Mild.</p>
<p>Reduce # under local anaesthesia(LA).</p>
<p>TYPES Band III: ANGULATED AND MALROTATED or POSTERIORLY DISPLACED.</p>
<ul>
<li>Severe swelling</li>
<li>Often unstable</li>
<li>Difficult to reduce</li>
<li>High risks of neurovascular injury</li>
<li>Circulatory compromise due to swelling.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="emergency-opration-in-theatre">EMERGENCY OPRATION IN THEATRE.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-opration-in-theatre" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>oNonoperative</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>long arm casting with less than 90 of elbow flexion</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>warm perfused hand without neuro deficits and</li>
<li>Type I (non-displaced) fractures</li>
<li>Type II fractures that meet the following criteria</li>
<li>anterior humeral line intersects the capitellum</li>
<li>minimal swelling present</li>
<li>no medial comminution</li>
<li>technique</li>
<li>typically used for 3 weeks</li>
<li>repeat radiographs at 1 week to assess for interval displacement</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>oOperative</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>closed reduction and percutaneous pinning (CRPP)</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>fracture pattern</li>
<li>type II and III supracondylar fractures</li>
<li>flexion type</li>
<li>medial column collapse</li>
<li>time to CRPP dictated by neurovascular status</li>
<li>non-urgent (can wait overnight)</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>warm perfused hand without neuro deficits</li>
<li>some argue can treat an isolated AIN injury in non-urgent fashion</li>
<li>technique</li>
<li>splint in 30-40 elbow flexion, admit overnight for observation and</li>
<li>elevation for elective surgery</li>
<li>urgent (same day - do not wait overnight)</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>pulseless, well-perfused hand</li>
<li>sensory nerve deficits</li>
<li>excessive swelling</li>
<li>"brachialis sign"</li>
<li>ecchymosis, dimpling/puckering antecubital fossa, palpable</li>
<li>subcutaneous bone fragment</li>
<li>indicates proximal fragment buttonholed through</li>
<li>brachialis</li>
<li>implies more serious injury, higher likelihood of arterial injury,</li>
<li>significant swelling, more difficult closed reduction</li>
<li>"floating elbow"</li>
</ul>
<p>38</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>ipsilateral supracondylar humerus and forearm/wrist fractures warrant timely pinning of both fractures to decrease the risk of compartment syndrome</p>
<p>technique
check vascular status after reduction
if evidence of good distal perfusion admit for 48 hours of observation</p>
<p>if not well perfused perform vascular exploration
emergent (within hours)
indications
pulseless, poorly perfused hand
emergent vascular exploration and CRPP
open reduction, percutaneous pinning, +/- vascular exploration
indications
open fracture
failed closed reduction
pulseless white OR pink hand that is unable to be reduced or there remains a gap</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="indications-for-operation">Indications for operation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#indications-for-operation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Fracture which cannot be reduced closed.</li>
<li>An open fracture (COMPOUND).</li>
<li>Fracture associated with vascular damage.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="continuous-traction">CONTINUOUS TRACTION.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#continuous-traction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Traction through a screw in the olecranon, with the arm held overhead can be used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-anteriorly-displaced-fracture">TREATMENT OF ANTERIORLY DISPLACED FRACTURE.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-anteriorly-displaced-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a rare injury (less than 5% of supracondylar fractures).</p>
<p>&gt; Stable---reduction and a posterior slab is bandaged for 3 weeks.
&gt; Unstable---percutaneous pins are used.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pin migration
 most common complication (~2%)</p>
<p>Infection
 occurs in 1-2.4%
 increased risk in age &lt; 4.5 years</p>
<p>39</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li> atypically superficial and treated with oral antibiotics</li>
<li>Cubitus valgus</li>
<li> caused by fracture malunion</li>
<li> can lead to tardy ulnar nerve palsy</li>
<li>Cubitus varus (gunstock deformity)</li>
<li> caused by fracture varus malunion, especially in medial comminution pattern</li>
<li> is NOT caused by growth disturbance</li>
<li> may represent a cosmetic issue with little functional limitations, however has been associated with posterolateral elbow instability</li>
<li> can lead to tardy ulnar nerve palsy</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recurvatum">Recurvatum<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recurvatum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li> common with non-operative treatment of Type II and Type III fractures</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nerve-palsy-from-injury">Nerve palsy from injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nerve-palsy-from-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li> usually resolve, nerves rarely torn</li>
<li> extension type fractures</li>
<li>neuropraxia in 11%</li>
<li>most commonly AIN</li>
<li>mechanism = tenting of nerve on fracture, or entrapment in the fracture site</li>
<li> flexion type fractures</li>
<li>neuropraxia in 17%</li>
<li>most commonly cause ulnar neuropraxia</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vascular-injury">Vascular Injury<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vascular-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p> radial pulse absent on initial presentation in 7-12%</p>
</li>
<li>
<p> pulseless hand after closed reduction and pinning (3-4%)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>if perfusion is lost following reduction and pinning, pins should be removed immediately</p>
</li>
<li>
<p> decision to explore is based on quality of extremity perfusion rather than absence of pulse</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="volkmann-ischemic-contracture">Volkmann ischemic contracture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#volkmann-ischemic-contracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li> rare, but dreaded complication</li>
<li> may result from elbow hyperflexion casting</li>
<li>increase in deep volar forearm compartment pressures and loss of radial pulse with elbow flexed &gt;90</li>
<li> rarely seen with CRPP and postoperative immobilization in less than 90</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="postoperative-stiffness">Postoperative stiffness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#postoperative-stiffness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li> rare after casting or after pinning procedures</li>
<li>remove pins and allow gentle ROM at 3-4 weeks postop</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="dislocation-of-the-elbow-joint">DISLOCATION OF THE ELBOW JOINT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dislocation-of-the-elbow-joint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Dislocation of the ulna-humeral joint is fairly common in adults than in children.</p>
<p>Elbow Dislocations are common elbow injuries which can be characterized as simple or complex depending on associated injury to nearby structures.</p>
<p>Diagnosis can be made with plain radiographs. CT studies can be helpful to evaluate for loose bodies or for surgical planning.</p>
<p>Treatment is closed reduction followed by a short period of immobilization for stable simple elbow dislocations. Surgical management is indicated for complex elbow dislocations associated with fractures or persistent instability.</p>
<p>The injuries are usually classified according to the direction of displacement.</p>
<p>However, 90% of cases the radio-ulnar complex is displaced posteriorly or posterior laterally often together with fractures of the restraining bony process.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury-and-pathology">MECHANISM OF INJURY AND PATHOLOGY:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury-and-pathology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A fall on the outstretched hand with the elbow in extension.</p>
<p>Provided there is no associated fracture reduction will usually be stable and recurrent dislocation is unlikely.</p>
<p>Combination of ligamentous disruption and fracture of the radial head, coranoid process or olecranon process (or worse still several fractures) will render the joint more unstable and liable to re-dislocation.</p>
<p>Once posterior dislocation has taken place lateral shift may also occur. Soft tissue disruption is often considerable and surrounding nerves and vessels may be damaged.</p>
<p>High energy injuries do not follow any rules. The so-called side- swipe injury which occur in cases when a car drivers elbow protruding through the window is struck by another vehicle. The result is forward dislocation with fractures of any or all the bones around the elbow, soft tissue damage (including neuro- vascular injury) is usually severe.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Pain</li>
</ol>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Patient supports his forearm with the elbow in slight flexion.</li>
<li>Obvious deformity</li>
<li>Swelling</li>
<li>The bony landmarks (olecranon and epicondyles) may be palpable and abnormally placed.</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>NOTE.</strong></p>
<p>Examine for signs of vascular and nerve damage.</p>
<p><strong>Imaging:</strong></p>
<p>X-ray Ap/lateral views to confirm the diagnosis.</p>
<p><strong>TREATMENT:</strong></p>
<p><strong>Uncomplicated Dislocation:</strong></p>
<p><strong>GA</strong></p>
<p>Reduce-pull the forearm with elbow slightly flexed</p>
<p>Test for full elbow joint full range movement.</p>
<p>Check for distal nerves and circulation.</p>
<p>Arm is held in a collar and cuff with elbow flexed above 90 degrees.</p>
<p>After one week the patient gently exercises his elbow.</p>
<p>At 3 weeks the collar and cuff is discarded</p>
<p>Elbow movements are allowed to return spontaneously and are never forced.</p>
<p><strong>Closed reduction and immobilization with early motion</strong></p>
<p><strong>closed reduction</strong></p>
<p><strong>otechnique</strong></p>
<p>ensure patient has sufficient analgesia to allow for adequate muscle relaxation</p>
<p>reduction maneuver requires a combination of:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>inline traction to improve coronal displacement</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>forearm supination to shift the coronoid under the trochlea</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>elbow flexion while placing direct pressure on tip of olecranon</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>a palpable "clunk" can be appreciated after most reductions</p>
<p>assess post reduction stability</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>elbow is often unstable in extension</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>elbow is often unstable to valgus stress</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>42</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>test by stressing elbow with forearm in pronation to lock the lateral side</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-24b98bc9.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-ccd988d7.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b9088e76.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg"></p>
<p>Included under anaesthesia as soon as possible</p>
<p>Reduction is usually easy</p>
<p>Pull steadily upon the forearm with the elbow semi-flexed, correct any sideways displacement, then apply direct pressure behind the olecranon to push it into the trochlear groove.</p>
<p>Reduction should be confirmed by radiographic examination and clinical tests.</p>
<p>Thereafter the elbow should be rested in a backslab cast in 90 degrees of flexion for 2-3 weeks, and thereafter mobilizing exercises.</p>
<p>DISLOCATION WITH ASSOCIATED FRACTURES.</p>
<p>Coronoid process.</p>
<p>Coronoid fractures have been classified by Regan and Morrey as:</p>
<p>Type I</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Avulsion of the tip. A benign enough injury, but it can represent a substantial soft tissue injury of the elbow.</p>
<p>Type II</p>
<p>A single or comminuted fracture of the coronoid with 50% or less involved.</p>
<p>This is usually not repaired surgically as the elbow remains stable.</p>
<p>Type III</p>
<p>A single or comminuted fracture involving more than 50%. If the elbow is unstable after reduction, then fixation is usually needed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medial-epicondyle">Medial Epicondyle:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medial-epicondyle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Reduce and the arm and wrist are splinted with elbow at 90 degrees after 3 weeks movements are began under supervision.</p>
<p>Head of Radius:</p>
<p>The combination of ligament disruption and a type II or III radial head fracture is unstable injury&gt;Fixation or prosthetic replacement of radial head.</p>
<p>Olecranon:</p>
<p>Open reduction with internal fixation is the best treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="side-swipe-injuries">SIDE-SWIPE INJURIES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#side-swipe-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Repair of vascular injury, skeletal stabilization and soft tissue coverage.</p>
<p>Persistent instability:</p>
<p>A hinged external fixator can be applied in order to maintain mobility while the tissue heal.</p>
<p>Complications:</p>
<p>Early.</p>
<p>44</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ol>
<li>Vascular injury</li>
<li>Nerve injury.</li>
</ol>
<p>Late.</p>
<ol>
<li>Stiffness</li>
<li>Heterotopic ossification (myositis ossificans)</li>
<li>Unreduced dislocation</li>
<li>Recurrent dislocation</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-around-the-elbow-in-children">FRACTURES AROUND THE ELBOW IN CHILDREN:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-around-the-elbow-in-children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most of these injuries are supracondylar fractures. Boys are injured more than girls and more than half of the patients are under the age of 10 years old.</p>
<p>A fall directly on the point of the elbow or more often onto the outstretched hand with the elbow forced into valgus or Varus. Pain and swelling are often marked and examination is difficult. X-ray interpretation also has problems: The bone ends are largely cartilaginous and therefore radiographically incompletely visualized. A good knowledge of the normal anatomy is essential. If fracture displacements are to be recognized.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="points-of-anatomy">POINTS OF ANATOMY:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#points-of-anatomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The elbow is a complex hinge-wide ranges of flexion, extension and rotation, yet also enough stability to support the necessary pushing, pulling and carrying activities of the daily life.</p>
<p>Liable to be compromised by any break in the articulating structures.</p>
<p>The surrounding soft tissue structures also are important, especially the capsular and collateral ligaments and to a lesser extent the muscles. Ligament disruption is also therefore a destabilizing factor.</p>
<p>Always compare the injured limb with the normal one.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Epiphyses and ossific centers should not be mistaken for fracture fragments on the x-rays.</p>
<p>The average ages at which the ossific centers appear are easily remembered by the</p>
<p>MNEMONIC: (different authors give different yrs of ossification{1-11, 1-12)</p>
<p>C-capitulum(1yr)</p>
<p>R-radial head (3yrs)</p>
<p>I-internal (medial epicondyle) 5yrs</p>
<p>T-trochlea(7yrs)</p>
<p>O-olecranon(9yrs)</p>
<p>E-external (lateral epicondyle) 11yrs</p>
<p>Obviously epiphyseal displacements will not be detected on X-ray before these ages.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-head-neck-radius">FRACTURE HEAD &amp; NECK RADIUS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-head-neck-radius" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Radial head fractures are common in adults but are hardly ever seen in</p>
<p>children (probably because the proximal radius is mainly (cartilaginous)</p>
<p>whereas radial neck fractures occur in children more frequently.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury">Mechanism of injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Can occur during elbow dislocation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>A fall on the outstretched hand with elbow extended and forearm</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>pronated, causes impaction of the radial head against the capitulum.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical features:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Pain on pronation and supination.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Tenderness on pressure over the radial head.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Swelling.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">Classification:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>By Mason.</p>
<p>46</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>a) Type I-An undisplaced vertical split in the radial head.
b) Type II-A displaced single fragment of the head.
c) Type III-The head broken into several fragments(comminuted).
d) Type IV-Fractures with elbow dislocation.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-9246e0ec.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="imaging">Imaging:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>X-ray PA/Lateral views.</p>
<p>Ct-scan in complicated fractures requiring operative management</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Type I.</p>
<p>Relief pain</p>
<p>Aspiration of haematoma under local anaesthesia(LA)</p>
<p>Arm is held in a collar and cuff for 3 weeks</p>
<p>Prognosis is good.</p>
<p>Type II</p>
<p>Reduction and use of one or two small headless screws.</p>
<p>Type III</p>
<p>Reconstruction with small headless screws or replaced with a metal spacer.</p>
<p>Assess always for an associated soft tissue injury.</p>
<p>I) Rupture of the medial collateral ligament.</p>
<p>II) Rupture of the interosseous membrane (ESSEX LOPRESTI LESION).</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>III) Combined fractures of the radial head and coronoid process plus dislocation of the elbow's the terrible triad.</p>
<p>Complications:</p>
<ol>
<li>Joint stiffness</li>
<li>Myositis ossificans</li>
<li>Recurrent instability of the elbow.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-of-the-radial-neck">FRACTURE OF THE RADIAL NECK.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-of-the-radial-neck" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In adults a displaced fracture of the radial neck may need open reduction. A mini-plate can be applied, making sure not to damage the articular surface. An alternative is to use oblique headless screws.</p>
<p>There are 3 main types:
a) A chisel like split of neck.
b) A marginal fracture.
c) A comminuted fracture.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-6920eca0.jpeg" alt="img-39.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-the-olecranon">FRACTURES OF THE OLECRANON:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-the-olecranon" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>MECHANISMS OF INJURY:</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>The olecranon is fractured by a fall onto the point of the elbow, especially in adults.</p>
<p>Olecranon fractures typically involve the articular surface of the elbow.</p>
<p>The triceps muscle inserts onto the olecranon, blending with the periosteum.</p>
<p>A displaced fracture interrupts the extensor mechanism resulting in loss of active elbow extension.</p>
<p>The pull from the triceps is the key deforming force, pulling the separated fragment superiorly.</p>
<p>Greater displacement suggests tearing of the fibrous sheath over the olecranon.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The fracture may take three forms:</p>
<ol>
<li>A crack without displacement</li>
<li>A clean break with separation of the two fragments</li>
<li>A comminuted fracture</li>
</ol>
<p>Two broad types of injury are seen;</p>
<ol>
<li>a comminuted fracture which is due to a direct blow or a fall on the elbow.</li>
<li>a transverse break due to traction when the patient falls onto the hand while the triceps muscle is contracted.</li>
</ol>
<p>The two types above can further be classified as&gt;a) displaced and b) undisplaced fracture</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b15d7f7e.jpeg" alt="img-40.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">Clinical features:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Pain</li>
<li>Bruise over the elbow.</li>
<li>Palpable gap at the fracture site.</li>
<li>Patient unable to extend the elbow against resistance.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="investigation">Investigation.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>x-ray</p>
<p>AP</p>
<p>Lateral view shows the details of the fracture.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Rest the arm in a sling for a week.</li>
<li>Active movements when the pain subsides.</li>
<li>Check x-ray after 2 weeks to ascertain healing.</li>
<li>Closed immobilization by a cast in about 60 degrees of flexion for 2-3 weeks then exercises are begun.</li>
<li>Operative treatment for displaced fractures.</li>
<li>Early mobilization is important to avoid joint stiffness.</li>
</ol>
<p>Depends upon the type of fracture as above:</p>
<p>Crack fracture:</p>
<p>The only treatment necessary is to protect the elbow by a light plaster splint for 2 or 3 weeks.</p>
<p>The plaster should be applied with the elbow held at 90 degrees. There is no fear of distracting the fragments as they are held together by the surrounding aponeurosis.</p>
<p>Clean break with separation:</p>
<p>It is hard to gain and hold perfect reduction by closed methods because the action of the triceps will angulate and distract the fragments.</p>
<p>Open reduction and internal fixation is the preferred choice of treatment.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>The fragments are exposed and fitted together accurately under direct vision, ensuring that the articular surface of the trochlear notch is perfectly smooth.</p>
<p>Clean fracture with separation...</p>
<p>Rigid fixation is attained by a long coarse-threaded cancellous screw passed down the bone from the upper surface of the olecranon.</p>
<p>Fixation can also be done by short stiff parallel K-wires driven vertically across the fracture, combined with a tensed figure-of-eight loop of wire (tension band wiring)</p>
<p>It may be advisable to protect the elbow in plaster for two weeks before mobilizing exercises are begun.</p>
<p>Comminuted fracture:</p>
<p>Perfect placement and fixation of the multiple fragments is not usually possible.</p>
<p>The usual treatment is therefore to excise the olecranon fragments by dissecting them out from the aponeurosis, then securing the triceps to the stump of the ulna by strong sutures passed through drill holes in the bone.</p>
<p>The elbow is then protected in a plaster for 3 weeks, followed by mobilization by active exercises</p>
<p>Transverse fracture fixation with screw and tension and wiring</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-6ea45a9c.jpeg" alt="img-41.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-3a600edb.jpeg" alt="img-42.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Stiffness</li>
<li>Non-union</li>
</ol>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Damage to ulna nerve.</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis as a late complication.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-forearm-bones-ulna-radius">FRACTURES OF FOREARM BONES (ULNA &amp; RADIUS):<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-forearm-bones-ulna-radius" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This covers the following:</p>
<ol>
<li>Monteggia fracture-dislocation</li>
<li>Galeazzi fracture-dislocation</li>
<li>Fractures shafts of radius and ulna</li>
<li>Colles fracture Smiths fracture</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="monteggia-fracture-dislocation">MONTEGGIA FRACTURE -DISLOCATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#monteggia-fracture-dislocation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is fracture of proximal third of the ulna associated with dislocation of the radial head</p>
<p>It is named after Giovanni Battista Monteggia</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms-of-injury">MECHANISMS OF INJURY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Forced pronation of the forearm</li>
<li>Direct blow on the back of upper forearm, as a person warding off an assault</li>
</ol>
<p>The radial head may dislocate anteriorly, laterally or posteriorly</p>
<p>Clinical features:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pain on the forearm</li>
<li>Swelling</li>
<li>Inability to supinate and pronate the forearm</li>
<li>Deformity</li>
</ul>
<p>Investigations</p>
<p>X-RAYS (A/P and lateral views)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reveal the fracture of ulna and the dislocation of radial head</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-cadf1786.jpeg" alt="img-43.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-7c060bcc.jpeg" alt="img-44.jpeg"></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Bado Classification</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Type I</td><td>Fracture of the proximal or middle third of the ulna with anterior dislocation of the radial head (most common in children and young adults)</td></tr><tr><td>Type II</td><td>Fracture of the proximal or middle third of the ulna with posterior dislocation of the radial head (70 to 80% of adult Monteggia fractures)</td></tr><tr><td>Type III</td><td>Fracture of the ulnar metaphysis (distal to coronoid process) with lateral dislocation of the radial head</td></tr><tr><td>Type IV</td><td>Fracture of the proximal or middle third of the ulna and radius with dislocation of the radial head in any direction</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol>
<li>Closed reduction by manipulation under G/A, then immobilize with an above elbow POP with the elbow joint at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>90</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em"></mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">90^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">9</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  for 6 weeks in adults and 4 weeks in children
Closed reduction succeeds in children but it's liable to fail in adults</li>
<li>Open reduction with internal fixation using plate and screws is the method of choice in Adults.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="operative-management">OPERATIVE MANAGEMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#operative-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>ORIF of ulna shaft fracture</li>
</ul>
<p>-indications</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>acute fractures which are open or unstable (long oblique)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>comminuted fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>most Monteggia fractures in adults are treated surgically</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>ORIF of ulna shaft fracture, open reduction of radial head</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>-indications</p>
<ul>
<li>failure to reduce radial head with ORIF of ulnar shaft only</li>
<li>ensure ulnar reduction is correct</li>
<li>complex injury pattern</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>IM Nailing of ulna</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>transverse or short oblique fracture</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Delayed union</li>
<li>Non union</li>
<li>Stiffness of the elbow joint</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis of radio-humeral joint</li>
<li>PIN neuropathy</li>
<li>up to 10% in acute injuries</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>observation for 2-3 months</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>spontaneously resolves in most cases</li>
<li>if no improvement obtain nerve conduction studies</li>
<li>Malunion with radial head dislocation</li>
<li>usually caused by failure to obtain anatomic alignment of ulna</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>ulnar osteotomy and open reduction of the radial head</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b6fe513f">GALEAZZI FRACTURE  DISLOCATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b6fe513f" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is fracture of shaft of radius (junction between mid and lower third)
with dislocation of inferior radio-ulna joint
It is named after Ricardo Galeazzi</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury">MECHANISM OF INJURY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Fall on outstretched hand with the elbow flexed</li>
<li>Direct blow
The head of ulna may be shifted medially, anteriorly or posteriorly</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pain on the forearm
Swelling
Inability to pronate and supinate the forearm due to pain at the inferior
radio-ulna joint
Deformity
Tenderness</p>
<p>54</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="investigation">INVESTIGATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>X-RAY (A/P and lateral views)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reveals the fracture of radius and dislocation of inferior radio-ulna joint</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-64a5d0a9.jpeg" alt="img-45.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ota-classification-of-radius-ulna">OTA classification of radius/ulna<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ota-classification-of-radius-ulna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>22-A2.3</th><th>Radius/ulna, diaphyseal, simple fracture of radius with dislocation of DRUJ</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>22-A3.3</td><td>Radius/ulna, diaphyseal, simple fracture of both bones (distal zone radius) with dislocation of DRUJ</td></tr><tr><td>22-B2.3</td><td>radius/ulna, diaphyseal, wedge fracture of radius with dislocation of DRUJ</td></tr><tr><td>22-B3.3</td><td>radius/ulna, diaphyseal, wedge of both bones with dislocation of DRUJ</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tratment">TRATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tratment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Closed reduction by manipulation under G/A, then immobilize with an above elbow POP with the elbow joint at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>90</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em"></mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">90^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">9</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  with the forearm supinated for 6 weeks in adults and 4 weeks in children. Closed reduction succeeds in children but is liable to fail in adults</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Open reduction with internal fixation using plate and screws- It is the method of choice in adults</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="operative-management">OPERATIVE MANAGEMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#operative-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>ORIF of radius with reduction and stabilization of DRUJ</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>all cases, as anatomic reduction of DRUJ is required</li>
<li>acute operative treatment far superior to late reconstruction</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Delayed union</li>
<li>Nonunion- occurs due to inadequate reduction</li>
<li>Stiffness of wrist and elbow joints</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis of inferior radio-ulna joint</li>
<li>Compartment syndrome: high energy crush injury, open fractures, vascular injuries or coagulopathies</li>
<li>Neurovascular injury</li>
<li>Refracture: removing plate too early (minimum 18 months), large plates (4.5mm), comminuted fractures, persistent radiographic lucency</li>
<li>Malunion</li>
<li>DRUJ subluxation</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="colle-s-fracture">COLLE'S FRACTURE:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#colle-s-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It was described by Professor Abraham Colles in 1842</p>
<p>It is fracture of the lower end of radius, about 2cm above the wrist joint associated with the following features:</p>
<ul>
<li>Impaction of fracture fragments</li>
<li>Dorsal displacement of distal fragment</li>
<li>Radial(lateral) displacement of distal fragment</li>
<li>Backward angulation of distal fragment</li>
<li>Supination of the forearm</li>
<li> Avulsion fracture of Styloid process of the ulna</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="incidence">INCIDENCE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#incidence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is rare below the age of 40 years</p>
<p>Its common in elderly particularly in postmenopausal women, attributed to osteoporosis</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanism-of-injury">MECHANISM OF INJURY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanism-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The fracture is most commonly caused by a fall onto a hard surface and breaking the fall with the outstretched hand in pronation.</p>
<p>Colles fracture usually occurs about one inch proximal to the radio-carpal joint with posterior and lateral displacement of the distal fragment resulting in the characteristic "dinner fork" or "bayonet" like deformity.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Colles' fracture is a common fracture in people with osteoporosis, second only to vertebral fractures.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="characteristic-features">CHARACTERISTIC FEATURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#characteristic-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The classic Colles' fracture has the following characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Transverse fracture of the distal radius</li>
<li>2.5 cm (1 inch) proximal to the radio-carpal joint</li>
<li>Dorsal displacement/ tilt</li>
<li>Dorsal angulation</li>
<li>Radial tilt</li>
<li>Radial shortening</li>
<li>Loss of ulnar inclination</li>
<li>Radial angulation of the wrist</li>
<li>Dorsal displacement of the distal fragment</li>
<li>Comminution at the fracture site</li>
<li>Associated fracture of the ulnar styloid process in more than 60% of cases.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-presentation">CLINICAL PRESENTATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>History of fall on an outstretched hand</li>
<li>Pain, numbness, tenderness, bruising, deformity of wrist.</li>
<li>"Dinner Fork" Deformity</li>
<li>Swelling of the wrist</li>
<li>Increased angulation of the distal radius</li>
<li>Inability to grasp objects</li>
<li>Inability to move the wrist joint</li>
</ul>
<p>DEFORMITY (DINNER FORK IN COLLES #)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-d5d40254.jpeg" alt="img-46.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-e4680276.jpeg" alt="img-47.jpeg">
57</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="investigations">INVESTIGATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>X-RAYS(A/P and lateral views)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reveals the fracture, type and extent of displacement</li>
</ul>
<p>COLLES FRACTURE</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-7787705e.jpeg" alt="img-48.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-2e1674c9.jpeg" alt="img-49.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-e8ebcefc.jpeg" alt="img-50.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-a88fafa7.jpeg" alt="img-51.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-db4c3db9.jpeg" alt="img-52.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-ebbddd5c.jpeg" alt="img-53.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Management depends on the severity of the fracture.</p>
<p>An undisplaced fracture is treated with a below elbow cast alone.</p>
<p>The cast is applied with the distal fragment in palmar flexion and</p>
<p>ulnar deviation.</p>
<p>The cast is retained for six weeks.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>A fracture with mild angulation and displacement requires reduction by closed manipulation, followed by immobilization with a below elbow cast for six weeks.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-closed-reduction-by-manipulation-under-g-a">1. Closed reduction by manipulation under G/A<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-closed-reduction-by-manipulation-under-g-a" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
