<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="technique">Technique<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The hand is grasped and traction is applied in the length of the bone</li>
<li>The distal fragment is then pushed into place by pressing on the dorsum while manipulating the wrist into flexion, ulnar deviation and pronation</li>
</ul>
<p>Request an check X-ray to confirm reduction</p>
<p>Apply a below elbow POP for 6 WEEKS</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-003bd2d8.jpeg" alt="img-54.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="communications">COMMUNICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#communications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Patients frequently heal well with no complications.</p>
<p>Possible complications include:</p>
<p>Malunion with dinner-fork deformity</p>
<p>Persistent translation of the carpus</p>
<p>Shortening of radius</p>
<p>Stiffness of the wrist</p>
<p>Carpal tunnel syndrome (due to median nerve compression)</p>
<p>Suddeck's atrophy (reflex sympathetic dystrophy)</p>
<p>Ulnar and radial compression neuropathy</p>
<ol>
<li>Compression of median nerve- presents with numbness of the fingers. {1,2,3 &amp;small portion of 4.</li>
</ol>
<p>-If symptoms are marked (Neuroplaxia/mildest form of traumatic peripheral nerve injury); open reduction to free the nerve is indicated</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Reflex sympathetic dystrophy/ Sudeck's post traumatic osteodystrophy</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>It is a poorly understood condition, thought to occur as result of disturbance of centrally mediated autonomic regulation with consequent increased stimulation of sympathetic and motor efferent fibres</li>
<li>Presents with painful, swollen and stiff fingers</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>Physiotherapy</li>
<li>Intravenous blockade with guanethidine sulphate for refractory symptoms</li>
</ul>
<p>Weakness of the wrist joint</p>
<p>Delayed rupture of extensor policis longus tendon-</p>
<p>Caused either by the fracture or POP.</p>
<p>Usual time between the time the fracture occurred and the rupture is 4-8 weeks</p>
<ul>
<li>Presents with inability to actively extend the thumb</li>
<li>Treatment is by tendon transfer of extensor indicis</li>
</ul>
<p>Subluxation of distal radio-ulna joint</p>
<ul>
<li>Presents with impaired wrist movements especially adduction (ulna deviation) and rotation</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>Minor cases- observation and follow up</li>
<li>Troublesome cases- Darroch's operation i.e., excision of the lower end of the ulna</li>
</ul>
<p>CLASSIFICATION:</p>
<p>Frykman Classification
Colles's fracture can be classified into eight different types based on the extra- or intra-articular nature of fractures.
Type I: transverse metaphyseal fracture. (Includes both Colles and Smith fractures as angulation is not a feature)
Type II: type I + ulnar styloid fracture
Type III: fracture involves the radiocarpal joint includes both Barton and reverse Barton fractures
Type IV: type III + ulnar styloid fracture
Type V: transverse fracture involves distal radioulnar joint
Type VI: type V + ulnar styloid fracture</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Type VII: comminuted fracture with the involvement of both the radiocarpal and radioulnar joints</p>
<p>Type VIII: type VII + ulnar styloid fracture</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5fcc9aaf.jpeg" alt="img-55.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="smiths-fracture">SMITHS FRACTURE:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#smiths-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Fracture of the distal radius with anterior displacement. Also called reverse Colles' fracture.</p>
<p>It is caused by a fall on the back of the hand with the wrist flexed.</p>
<p>It is a fracture of distal radius with features opposite those of Colle's fracture i.e., the distal fragment is displaced forward and rotated forward</p>
<p>It a rare fracture</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms-of-injury">MECHANISMS OF INJURY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Backward fall of an outstretched hand</li>
<li>Fall on the dorsum of the hand in a flexed wrist joint</li>
</ol>
<p>Clinical features:</p>
<p>Pain on the wrist joint</p>
<p>Swelling around the wrist joint</p>
<p>Inability to move the wrist joint</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Instead, there is a garden spade deformity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="smith-fracture">Smith fracture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#smith-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b2dc75c1.jpeg" alt="img-56.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="investigation">INVESTIGATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#investigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="x-rays-a-p-and-lateral-views">X-RAYS(A/P and lateral views)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#x-rays-a-p-and-lateral-views" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Reveals the fracture and the degree of displacement</li>
<li>There is a fracture through the distal radial metaphysis.</li>
<li>A lateral view shows the distal fragment displaced and tilted anteriorly  the opposite of Colles fracture.</li>
<li>The entire metaphysis can be fractured or there can be an oblique fracture exiting at the dorsal or volar rim of the radius.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-baf37162.jpeg" alt="img-57.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The fracture is reduced by traction, supination and extension of the wrist, and the forearm is immobilized in a cast for 6 weeks.</p>
<p>Check X-rays should be taken after 7-10 days to ensure the fracture has not re-displaced.</p>
<p>Unstable fractures should be fixed with percutaneous wires or a plate.</p>
<ol>
<li>Closed reduction by manipulation under G/A</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Technique is the reverse of that of Colles fracture</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Then immobilize with a below elbow POP with the forearm in supination for 6 weeks</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Open reduction with internal fixation using a Buttress plate</li>
</ol>
<p>Indications:
a) Severely displaced fractures
b) Pressure to radial artery</p>
<p>Complications:</p>
<ol>
<li>Pressure to radial artery</li>
<li>Malunion</li>
<li>Stiffness of the wrist and fingers</li>
<li>Weakness of the wrist joint</li>
<li>Post traumatic arthritis</li>
</ol>
<p>BARTONS FRACTURE:</p>
<p>Intra-articular fracture of the distal radius.</p>
<p>A Barton's fracture is an intra-articular fracture of the distal radius with subluxation or dislocation of the radiocarpal joint.</p>
<p>There exist two types of Barton's fracture - dorsal and palmar, the latter being more common.</p>
<p>Barton's fracture is caused by a fall on an extended and pronated wrist increasing carpal compression force on the dorsal rim.</p>
<p>Intra-articular component distinguishes this fracture from a Smith's or a Colles' fracture.</p>
<p>Treatment of this fracture is usually done by open reduction and internal fixation with a plate and screws.</p>
<p>Occasionally the fracture can be treated conservatively, but it easily gets re-displaced.</p>
<p>63</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>

<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-efa4d339.jpeg" alt="img-59.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-c669bfbf.jpeg" alt="img-60.jpeg">
Displaced Barton's volar type distal left wrist radius fracture</p>
<p>Illustrations By: HUGH THOMAS, NEW YORK, NEW YORK</p>
<p>DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS:</p>
<ul>
<li>Colles's fracture</li>
<li>Smith's fracture</li>
<li>Die-punch fractureis a fracture of articular surface of the radius with depression of lunate facet.</li>
<li>The chauffer's #avulsion fracture of radial styloid process</li>
</ul>
<p>Complications:</p>
<ul>
<li>Carpal tunnel syndrome</li>
<li>Radial nerve compression</li>
<li>Complex regional pain</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="post-traumatic-arthritis">Post traumatic arthritis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#post-traumatic-arthritis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="malunion">Malunion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#malunion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="wrist-hand-fractures">WRIST &amp; HAND FRACTURES.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#wrist-hand-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>classification</p>
<p><strong>Injuries of the carpus</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Fracture of the scaphoid bone</li>
<li>Fracture of other carpal bones</li>
<li>Dislocation of the carpal bones</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Injuries of the metacarpal bones and phalanges</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Fracture of the base of the first metacarpal</li>
<li>Other fractures of the metacarpal bones</li>
<li>Fractures of the phalanges</li>
<li>Dislocations of the metacarpo-phalangeal and interphalangeal joints</li>
</ul>
<p>Strain of the interphalangeal joints</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-scaphoid-bone">Fractures of scaphoid bone:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-scaphoid-bone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Scaphoid fractures account for about 75% of all carpal injuries.</p>
<ul>
<li>Fracture of the scaphoid bone is common in young adults.</li>
<li>The scaphoid lies obliquely across the two rows of carpal bones, and is also in the line of loading between the thumb and forearm.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms-of-injury">Mechanisms of injury:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Violent hyperextension of the wrist will crack the waist of the scaphoid across its narrowest point.</p>
<ul>
<li>The fracture nearly always occurs transversely through the middle, or waist, of the scaphoid.</li>
<li>The usual cause is a fall onto the outstretched hand.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathology">Pathology:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Most scaphoid fractures are stable. Usually there is no displacement of the fragments, which lie in close apposition.</li>
<li>If displacement occurs and is allowed to persist, it causes a 'step' between the fracture fragments and favors the development of degenerative arthritis.</li>
<li>Problems associated with fracture scaphoid:</li>
<li>There are very few reliable clinical signs</li>
<li>The fracture can go on to non-union if it is not immobilized</li>
<li>It is not easily seen on initial radiographs, even if several views are taken</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Because the blood supply to the proximal pole of the bone enters by the distal pole in most people, the proximal fragment can become devitalized and undergo avascular necrosis.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-fa44967c.jpeg" alt="img-61.jpeg"></p>
<p>There is no deformity, crepitus or bruising around a fractured scaphoid.</p>
<p>The appearance may be deceptively normal</p>
<p>Features include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Swelling (fullness) in the anatomical snuff box</li>
<li>Tenderness in the anatomical snuff box</li>
<li>Weakness of pinch</li>
<li>Pain on hyperextension of the wrist / thumb</li>
<li>Pain on proximal pressure along the axis of the thumb</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="x-ray-findings">x-ray findings:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#x-ray-findings" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Antero-posterior, lateral and oblique views are all essential.</p>
<p>A fresh fracture may be seen only in the oblique view</p>
<p>Usually, the fracture line is transverse and through the narrowest part (waist) of the bone</p>
<p>Sometimes only the tubercle of the scaphoid is fractured.</p>
<p>When the clinical features suggest fracture of the scaphoid bone but the initial radiographs give no confirmation of it, radiographic examination should be repeated after an interval of two weeks.</p>
<p>A fracture may sometimes become obvious after an interval even though it was not apparent in the initial films.</p>
<p>In the interval it is advisable to support the injured wrist in a plaster.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The fracture is immobilized in a cast for 8-12 weeks [2-3 months].</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Since there is usually no displacement, reduction is not required.</p>
<p>The plaster used for scaphoid fracture is slightly more extensive than that used for a fracture of the lower end of the radius.</p>
<p>It should extend down the thumb to the level of the interphalangeal joint and it must be moulded firmly round the first metacarpal bone.</p>
<p>The palm is left free beyond the proximal skin crease to allow a full range of movement at the metacarpophalangeal joints of fingers, and the thumb should be free to move at the interphalangeal joint.</p>
<p>The cast must hold the thumb roughly opposite the ring finger and not in wide abduction because this interferes with function and could displace the fragments.</p>
<p>If the fracture is not united by 12 weeks, internal fixation (by a special compression screw [Herbert]) and grafting should be done.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="scaphoid-cast">Scaphoid cast:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#scaphoid-cast" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-5f6d31a4.jpeg" alt="img-62.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">Complications:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Avascular necrosis</li>
<li>Delayed union</li>
<li>Non-union</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis of the wrist</li>
<li>Delayed union:</li>
<li>Is common</li>
<li>May still be un-united 4-6 months after injury</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>If union has not occurred within 4 months despite continuous rest in plaster, the plaster should be removed, and mobilizing exercises started.</li>
<li>If the wrist remains uncomfortable and much restricted in function, operation should be done.</li>
<li>Fixation with a special compression screw with cancellous bone graft.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-union">Non-union:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-union" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Could be due to: -</li>
<li>Imperfect immobilization</li>
<li>Action of synovial fluid hindering the formation of an initial fibrinous bridge between the fragments</li>
<li>Impairment of blood supply to one of the fragments</li>
<li>Avascular necrosis of the proximal fragment</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Internal fixation by a compression screw and cancellous bone graft.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="avascular-necrosis-of-the-proximal-fragment">Avascular necrosis of the proximal fragment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#avascular-necrosis-of-the-proximal-fragment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The blood supply to the proximal fragment is interrupted by fracture through the waist of the scaphoid because the main nutrient vessels enter the bone through the distal half of the bone.</p>
<p>If the remaining blood supply is inadequate, the proximal fragment may die.</p>
<p>Avascular necrosis may be diagnosed from the radiographic appearance, because the avascular bone does not share in the general disuse osteoporosis of the carpal bones, and it therefore stands in sharp contrast to the other bones by virtue of its relatively greater density. This appearance manifests about 1-3 months after injury.</p>
<p><strong>Treatment:</strong>
Once arthritic changes develop no treatment can restore the wrist to normal.</p>
<p><strong>Osteoarthritis:</strong>
Occurs due to non-union or avascular necrosis</p>
<p><strong>Treatment:</strong>
Often unsatisfactory</p>
<ul>
<li>Protective wrist support</li>
<li>Arthrodesis of the wrist</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dislocation-of-lunate-bone">DISLOCATION OF LUNATE BONE:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dislocation-of-lunate-bone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The lunate is liable to dislocation through a fall onto the hand.</p>
<p>The lunate bone is somewhat wedge-shaped with its base anteriorly, and with the hand extended in a fall, the bone may be squeezed out from between the capitate bone and the radius.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>The displacement in a lateral radiograph is characteristic: the lunate bone lies at the front of the wrist and is rotated through 90 degrees or more on a horizontal axis so that its concave lower articular surface faces forwards.</p>
<p>For it to dislocate to that position, its posterior ligamentous attachments must be torn, so that it hinges on the anterior ligaments.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-9e373cf7.jpeg" alt="img-63.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-43c560e8.jpeg" alt="img-64.jpeg">
Normal</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b3d5e61f.jpeg" alt="img-65.jpeg">
Lunate Dislocation</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">Treatment:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Attempt closed manipulative reduction of the dislocation under anaesthesia.</p>
<p>Strong traction is first applied to the hand to open up the space for the lunate bone.</p>
<p>Direct pressure is then applied over the displaced bone and it may pop back into position.</p>
<p>The position should then be stabilized with percutaneous Kirschner wires, then immobilized by cast to avoid redisplacement.</p>
<p>The cast and wires are retained for 4 weeks before active mobilizing exercises are begun.</p>
<p>If manipulation is unsuccessful, the bone should be replaced (repositioned/ reduced) at operation, and the carpus stabilized with Kirschner wires</p>
<p>In old neglected cases, it may be necessary to excise the bone rather than attempt to replace it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complication-of-lunate-dislocation">Complication of lunate dislocation:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complication-of-lunate-dislocation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="avascular-necrosis">Avascular necrosis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#avascular-necrosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The blood supply of the displaced lunate is precarious because many of the soft tissue attachments are torn.</p>
<p>There is therefore, risk of avascular necrosis.</p>
<p>Signs of avascular necrosis may appear within 1-4 months.</p>
<p>X-rays [or MRI scanning] should be taken at 2-monthly intervals to observe the condition of the lunate bone after reduction of the dislocation</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="osteoarthritis">Osteoarthritis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#osteoarthritis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Will occur inevitably if the lunate bone is avascular and it is not excised.</p>
<p>Treatment should be conservative at first  leather or plastic wrist support</p>
<p>If the disability becomes severe, the only satisfactory treatment is by arthrodesis of the wrist.</p>
<p>Injury to the median nerve:</p>
<p>The median nerve can be injured from direct pressure of the displaced bone. It is liable to be trapped between the displaced lunate bone and the flexor retinaculum.</p>
<p>If nerve injury occurs, the nerve must be decompressed as soon as possible by reduction of the lunate dislocation.</p>
<p>At the same time the flexor retinaculum should be divided to decompress the carpal tunnel.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-metacarpal">FRACTURE METACARPAL:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-metacarpal" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Base 1 metacarpal # (bennets Rolando)</p>
<p>A fracture of the base of the first metacarpal bone is usually sustained from forced abduction of the thumb, or longitudinal violence applied by a blow, as in boxing.</p>
<p>Clinically there is local tenderness distal to the anatomical snuff box, unlike a scaphoid fracture, and this may be accompanied by deformity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathology">PATHOLOGY:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are 2 distinct types of this injury:</p>
<ol>
<li>A transverse or short oblique fracture across the base of the metacarpal, but not entering the joint</li>
<li>An oblique fracture entering the carpo-metacarpal joint at about the middle of the articular surface (Bennetts fracture-subluxation).</li>
</ol>
<p>The second type is the more serious, because unless a smooth surface can be restored, osteoarthritis will develop later.</p>
<p>When the fracture is oblique there is a tendency for the large distal fragment to be displaced backwards and upwards upon the small proximal fragment due to the pull of the abductor pollicis longus tendon.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bennette">BENNETTE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bennette" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-4722e5af.jpeg" alt="img-66.jpeg"></p>
<p>Structure involving the base of the thumb.</p>
<p>Defined as intraarticular simple, oblique fracture of the first metacarpal base.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Manipulative reduction under anaesthesia.</li>
<li>Often difficult to maintain the reduction in the oblique Bennett fracture.</li>
<li>A well-moulded cast should be applied (including the forearm and wrist), holding the thumb metacarpal well extended at the carpo-metacarpal joint.</li>
<li>Check radiographs are taken twice during the first week to determine whether a good position has been maintained.</li>
<li>Reduction should be accurate for fractures involving the joint.</li>
<li>Therefore, operation is advised in the majority of intra-articular fractures.</li>
</ul>
<p>Open reduction and internal fixation of the fragments with a small screw</p>
<ul>
<li>An alternative method is to use percutaneous Kirschner wire after the fracture is first reduced by traction combined with thumb pressure against the displaced base of the metacarpal.</li>
<li>The K-wire is driven obliquely through the base of the metacarpal into the trapezium.</li>
<li>After fixation by either method a well-fitting cast should be retained for 4-6 weeks.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complication">COMPLICATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li><strong>Osteoarthritis:</strong></li>
<li>Likely to follow intra-articular fractures that have left the joint surface irregular.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="other-metacarpal-fractures">OTHER METACARPAL FRACTURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-metacarpal-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Are fairly common at all ages</p>
<p>The most common causes are a fall onto the hand or a blow on the knuckles as in boxing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>May be classified according to site:</p>
<ul>
<li>Fracture through the base of the metacarpal  transverse and undisplaced</li>
<li>Fracture through the shaft  transverse or oblique.</li>
<li>A transverse fracture may be undisplaced, or may be displaced with overlap of fragments</li>
<li>An oblique fracture tends to allow telescoping with consequent shortening and recession of the knuckle.</li>
<li>Fracture through the neck of the metacarpal  may be undisplaced.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-e95aa34e.jpeg" alt="img-67.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Depends upon the degree of displacement.</p>
<p>Undisplaced fractures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Includes fractures with acceptable displacement</li>
<li>The position is stable</li>
<li>Sound union and perfect recovery of function may be expected without treatment.</li>
<li>Provide temporary support to relieve pain and apprehension.</li>
<li>Apply a light dorsal plaster slab for 3 weeks.</li>
<li>Finger movements and active use should be encouraged from the beginning.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Displaced fractures:</p>
<p>Reduction of the displacement and maintenance of the reduction are required.</p>
<p>May require manual reduction and external splintage or operation, depending on the nature of the individual fracture.</p>
<p>Loss of end-to-end apposition of transverse #s may result in overlap and necessitate open reduction with intramedullary wire or miniature plate fixation.</p>
<p>Long oblique fractures may require transfixion wire of small fragment screws.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-the-phalanges">FRACTURES OF THE PHALANGES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-the-phalanges" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>May be of various patterns:</p>
<ol>
<li>Long spiral fracture of shaft</li>
<li>Oblique fracture of base</li>
<li>Transverse fracture of shaft</li>
<li>Comminuted fracture of distal phalanx</li>
<li>Mallet finger from avulsion fracture of base of distal phalanx</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Immobilization should be kept to a minimum</p>
<p>Nearly all phalangeal fractures proceed to bony union whether they are splinted or not.</p>
<p>The main purposes of splintage are:</p>
<p>To prevent redisplacement of fractures that have been reduced</p>
<p>To relieve pain</p>
<p>Splintage is maintained for 2-3 weeks</p>
<p>Undisplaced fractures of the shaft</p>
<p>The fragments are held together with periosteal sheath.</p>
<p>There is no fear of displacement.</p>
<p>Treatment is directed towards relief of pain</p>
<p>Bind the phalanges of the injured finger lightly to the corresponding segments of an adjacent normal finger with adhesive strapping.</p>
<p>The sound finger thus acts as support to the injured one  buddy splintage.</p>
<p>73</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Displaced fractures and fractures involving joint surfaces</p>
<p>Reduce the displacement by manipulation, and hold the position by a malleable splint.</p>
<p>Immobilize for not more than 3 weeks.</p>
<p>Start active exercises</p>
<p>If displacement cannot be controlled by splintage, operation may be required.</p>
<p>The fragments may be held in position by Kirschner wires inserted percutaneously after manipulation, or after open reduction.</p>
<p>Comminuted fracture of distal phalanx</p>
<p>The pulp or nail bed may be torn, and the nail may often separate.</p>
<p>The fracture should be ignored and attention directed solely to the soft tissue injury.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mallet-finger-baseball-finger">MALLET FINGER: (BASEBALL FINGER #)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mallet-finger-baseball-finger" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Is caused by sudden passive flexion of the distal interphalangeal joint, as in a ball striking the tip of the finger, rupturing the extensor tendon at the point of its insertion into the base of the distal phalanx.</p>
<p>Sometimes a fragment of bone is avulsed from the phalanx.</p>
<p>The distal interphalangeal joint rests in moderate flexion and cannot be actively extended.</p>
<p>There is tenderness over the site of avulsion.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-716e8955.jpeg" alt="img-68.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-f3d20604.jpeg" alt="img-69.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Tendon avulsion, with or without a small bone fragment, is treated by uninterrupted splintage in the fully straight position for 6 weeks.</p>
<p>Immobilization is confined to the distal interphalangeal joints, the proximal joint being left free.</p>
<p>Larger avulsed fragment that is displaced may be reduced at operation and held in position by a K-wire passed through the distal phalanx and the reduced fragment, and across the joint to hold it fully extended.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mallet-finger-splint">MALLET FINGER SPLINT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mallet-finger-splint" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-39ad9607.jpeg" alt="img-70.jpeg"></p>
<p>AL &amp; INTERPHALANGEAL JOINTS:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mechanisms-of-injury">MECHANISMS OF INJURY:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mechanisms-of-injury" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Most dislocation of finger and thumb joints are caused by forced hyperextension.</p>
<p>The distal segment is usually displaced backwards from the proximal.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The dislocation should be reduced promptly.</p>
<p>Reduction is effected easily by pulling upon the digit and applying direct pressure over the base of the displaced phalanx.</p>
<p>Can often be done without anaesthesia.</p>
<p>Radiographs should be taken to check the reduction.</p>
<p>Immobilization is not required, and active movements of the joint should be encouraged from an early stage.</p>
<p>Button-hole injuries</p>
<p>Sometimes in metacarpo-phalangeal dislocations the head of the metacarpal bone is driven forwards through a rent in the front of the capsule at the same time as the phalanx is dislocated backwards.</p>
<p>In this case manipulative reduction may be impossible and operation is required.</p>
<p>The capsular slit is enlarged sufficiently to allow it to be hooked back over the metacarpal head.</p>
<p>75</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fractures-of-the-pelvic-ring">FRACTURES OF THE PELVIC RING:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-the-pelvic-ring" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Pelvic ring fractures are high energy fractures of the pelvic ring which typically occur due to blunt trauma or MVA.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made radiographically with pelvic radiographs and further characterized with CT scan.</p>
<p>Treatment is typically operative fixation depending on degree of pelvis instability, fracture displacement and patient activity demands.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Associated injuries</p>
<ul>
<li>orthopaedics</li>
<li>chest injury in up to 63%</li>
<li>long bone fractures in 50%</li>
<li>spine fractures in 25%</li>
<li>non-orthopaedic</li>
<li>urogenital</li>
<li>sexual dysfunction up to 50%</li>
<li>head and abdominal injury in 40%</li>
</ul>
<p>Pediatric pelvic ring fractures</p>
<ul>
<li>children with open triradiate cartilage have different fracture patterns than do children whose triradiate cartilage has closed</li>
<li>if triradiate cartilage is open the iliac wing is weaker than the elastic pelvic ligaments, resulting in bone failure before pelvic ring disruption for this reason fractures usually involve the pubic rami and iliac wings and rarely require surgical treatment</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anatomy">ANATOMY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anatomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Osteology</li>
<li>ring structure made up of the sacrum and two innominate bones</li>
<li>stability dependent on strong surrounding ligamentous structures</li>
<li>displacement can only occur with disruption of the ring in two places</li>
<li>neurovascular structures intimately associated with posterior pelvic ligaments</li>
<li>high index of suspicion for injury of internal iliac vessels or lumbosacral plexus</li>
</ul>
<p>76</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>oLigaments</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>anterior</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>symphyseal ligaments</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist external rotation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>pelvic floor</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>sacrospinous ligaments</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist external rotation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>sacrotuberous ligaments</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist shear and flexion</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>posterior sacroiliac complex (posterior tension band)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>strongest ligaments in the body</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>more important than anterior structures for pelvic ring stability</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>anterior sacroiliac ligaments</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist external rotation after failure of pelvic floor and anterior structures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>interosseous sacroiliac</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist anterior-posterior translation of pelvis</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>posterior sacroiliac</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist cephalad-caudad displacement of pelvis</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>iliolumbar</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>resist rotation and augment posterior SI ligaments</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>oVascular</p>
<ul>
<li>common iliac system begins near L4 at bifurcation of abdominal aorta</li>
<li>external iliac artery courses anteriorly along pelvic brim and emerges as the common femoral artery distal to the inguinal ligament</li>
<li>internal iliac artery dives posteriorly near SI joint and divides in the posterior division (giving of superior gluteal artery) and anterior division (becoming obturator artery)</li>
<li>corona mortis is a connection between the obturator and external iliac systems</li>
<li>mean distance of 6.2cm from the pubic symphysis</li>
<li>venous plexus in posterior pelvis accounts for 90% of the hemorrhage associated with pelvic ring injuries</li>
</ul>
<p>oNeurologic</p>
<ul>
<li>Lumbosacral trunk crosses anterior sacral ala and SI joint</li>
<li>L5 nerve root exits below L5 TP a courses over sacral ala 2cm medial to SI joint</li>
</ul>
<p>INCIDENCE:</p>
<ul>
<li>high energy blunt trauma</li>
<li>High mortality rate: 15-25% for closed fractures, 50% for open fractures</li>
<li>In paediatrics triradiate cartilage is open the iliac wing is weaker than the elastic pelvic ligaments, resulting in bone failure before pelvic ring disruption</li>
</ul>
<p>ASSOCIATED INJURIES:</p>
<p>77</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>chest injury in up to 63%</li>
<li>long bone fractures in 50%</li>
<li>sexual dysfunction up to 50%</li>
<li>head and abdominal injury in 40%</li>
<li>spine fractures in 25%</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physical-exam">PHYSICAL EXAM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-exam" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Symptoms</li>
<li>pain &amp; inability to bear weight</li>
<li>Physical exam</li>
<li>inspection</li>
<li>test stability by placing gentle rotational force on each iliac crest</li>
<li>low sensitivity for detecting instability</li>
<li>perform only once</li>
<li>look for abnormal lower extremity positioning</li>
<li>external rotation of one or both extremities</li>
<li>limb-length discrepancy</li>
<li>skin</li>
<li>scrotal, labial or perineal hematoma, swelling or ecchymosis</li>
<li>flank hematoma</li>
<li>lacerations of perineum</li>
<li>degloving injuries (Morel-Lavallee lesion)</li>
<li>neurologic exam</li>
<li>rule out lumbosacral plexus injuries (L5 and S1 are most common)</li>
<li>rectal exam to evaluate sphincter tone and perirectal sensation</li>
<li>up to 10-15% of patients will sustain neurologic injury</li>
<li>urogenital exam</li>
<li>most common finding is gross hematuria</li>
<li>more common in males (21% in males, 8% in females)</li>
<li>vaginal and rectal examinations</li>
<li>mandatory to rule out occult open fracture (HIDDEN)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="radiological-examination">RADIOLOGICAL EXAMINATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#radiological-examination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>AP Pelvis</li>
<li>part of initial ATLS evaluation</li>
<li>look for asymmetry, rotation or displacement of each hemipelvis</li>
<li>evidence of anterior ring injury needs further imaging</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tiles-classification">TILES CLASSIFICATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tiles-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>oTile classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oA: Stable</th><th>o</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>oA1: fracture not involving the ring (avulsion or iliac wing fracture)</td></tr><tr><td>o</td><td>o</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>A2: stable or minimally displaced fracture of the ring</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>A3: transverse sacral fracture (Denis zone III sacral fracture)</td></tr><tr><td>B: Rotationally unstable, vertically stable </td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>B1: open book injury (external rotation)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>B2: lateral compression injury (internal rotation)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>B2-1: with anterior ring rotation/displacement through ipsilateral o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>B2-2-with anterior ring rotation/displacement through contralateral handle injury)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>B3: bilateral</td></tr><tr><td>C: Rotationally and vertically unstable</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C1: unilateral</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C1-1: iliac fracture</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C1-2: sacroiliac fracture-dislocation</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C1-3: sacral fracture</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C2: bilateral with one side type B and one side type C</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>C3: bilateral with both sides type C</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="young-burgess-classification">YOUNG BURGESS CLASSIFICATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#young-burgess-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Anterior Posterior Compression (APC)</p>
<p>Lateral Compression (LC)</p>
<p>Vertical Shear (VS)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-02095b54.jpeg" alt="img-71.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="public-symphysis-diastasis">PUBLIC SYMPHYSIS DIASTASIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#public-symphysis-diastasis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Young-Burgess Classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Anterior Posterior Compression (APC)</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>APC I</td><td>Symphysis widening &lt; 2.5 cm</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Symphysis widening &gt; 2.5 cm. Anterior SI joint diastasis.</td></tr><tr><td>APC II</td><td>Posterior SI ligaments are intact. Disruption of sacrospinous and sacrotuberous ligaments.</td></tr><tr><td>APC III</td><td>Disruption of anterior and posterior SI ligaments (SI dislocation). Disruption of sacrospinous and sacrotuberous ligaments. APCIII associated with vascular injury</td></tr><tr><td></td><td> Lateral Compression (LC)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Oblique or transverse ramus fracture and ipsilateral anterior sacral compression fracture.</td></tr><tr><td>LC I</td><td>Rami fracture and ipsilateral posterior ilium fracture dislocation (cr fracture).</td></tr><tr><td>LC II</td><td>Ipsilateral lateral compression and contralateral APC (windswept pe Common mechanism is rollover vehicle accident or pedestrian vs a</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Vertical Shear (VS)</td></tr><tr><td>Posterior and superior directed force.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Vertical shear</td><td>Associated with the highest risk of hypovolemic shock (63%); mortality rat 25%</td></tr><tr><td>INITIAL MANAGEMENT &amp; RESUSITATION</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Bleeding Source intraabdominal (present in up to 40% of cases) intrathoracic retroperitoneal extremity (thigh compartments) pelvic common sources of hemorrhage venous injury (80%) shearing injury of posterior thin walled venous plexus</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>leads to retroperitoneal hematoma (can hold up to 4L of blood)</p>
<p>bleeding cancellous bone</p>
<p>uncommon sources of hemorrhage</p>
<p>arterial injury (10-20%)</p>
<p>superior gluteal most common (posterior ring injury, APC pattern)</p>
<p>internal pudendal (anterior ring injury, LC pattern)</p>
<p>obturator (LC pattern)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>o</strong>resuscitation</p>
<p>PRBC (PACKED RED BLOOD CELLS):FFP(FRESH FROZEN PLASMA):Platelets</p>
<p>ideally should be transfused 1:1:1</p>
<p>this ratio shown to improve mortality in patients requiring massive</p>
<p>transfusion</p>
<p><strong>o</strong>pelvic binder/sheet</p>
<p><strong>indications</strong></p>
<p>initial management of an unstable ring injury</p>
<p>should be centered over the greater trochanters</p>
<p><strong>contraindications</strong></p>
<p>hypothetical risk of over-rotation of hemipelvis and hollow viscus</p>
<p>injury (bladder) in pelvic fractures with internal rotation</p>
<p>component (LC)</p>
<p>no clinical evidence exists of this complication occurring</p>
<p><strong>pitfalls</strong></p>
<p>binder can mask pelvic ring injuries, creating false negative</p>
<p>radiographs and CT images</p>
<p>stress examination under anesthesia may be indicated in patients</p>
<p>who present to the trauma slot in a pelvic binder, hemodynamic</p>
<p>instability, and negative pelvis radiographs/CT scan</p>
<p><strong>o</strong>external fixation</p>
<p><strong>indications</strong></p>
<p>pelvic ring injuries with an external rotation component (APC,</p>
<p>VS, )</p>
<p>unstable ring injury with ongoing blood loss</p>
<p>should be placed before emergent laparotomy</p>
<p><strong>contraindications</strong></p>
<p>ilium fracture that precludes safe application</p>
<p>81</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>acetabular fracture</p>
<p>angiography / embolization</p>
<p>technique</p>
<p>selective embolization of identifiable bleeding sources</p>
<p>in patients with uncontrolled bleeding after selective</p>
<p>embolization, bilateral temporary internal iliac embolization may be effective</p>
<p>repeat angiography if patient continues to be hypotensive after embolization</p>
<p>recurrent hemorrhage from previously embolized artery is common</p>
<p>complications include gluteal necrosis</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="definitive-treatment">DEFINITIVE TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definitive-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>oOverview by Classification</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>Definitive treatment of Anterior Posterior Compression (APC) injuries</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>APC I</th><th>Non-operative. Protected weight bearing</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>APC II</td><td>Anterior symphyseal plate or external fixator +/- posterior fixation</td></tr><tr><td>APC III</td><td>Anterior symphyseal multi-hole plate or external fixator and posterior stabilization with SI screws or plate/screws</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Definitive treatment of Lateral Compression (LC) injuries</p>
<p>Non-operative.</p>
<p>|  LC I | -Protected weight bearing (complete, comminuted sacral component.
-Weight bearing as tolerated (simple, incomplete sacral fracture)  |
| --- | --- |
|  LC II | Open reduction and internal fixation of ilium  |
|   | Posterior stabilization with plate or SI screws as needed.  |
|  LC III | Percutaneous or open based on injury pattern and surgeon preference.  |</p>
<p>Definitive treatment of Vertical Shear (VS) injuries</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>|  Vertical Shear | Posterior stabilization with plate or SI screws as needed.
Percutaneous or open based on injury pattern and surgeon preference.  |
| --- | --- |
|  Nonoperative | weight bearing as tolerated
indications
mechanically stable pelvic ring injuries including
LC1
anterior impaction fracture of sacrum and
oblique ramus fractures with &lt; 1cm of
posterior ring displacement
APC1
traumatic widening of symphysis &lt; 2.5 cm
with intact posterior pelvic ring
isolated pubic ramus fractures
parturition-induced pelvic diastasis
bedrest and pelvic binder in acute setng
with diastasis less than 4cm  |
|  Operative | ORIF
indications
symphysis diastasis &gt; 2.5 cm
SI joint displacement &gt; 1 cm
sacral fracture with displacement &gt; 1 cm
displacement or rotation of hemipelvis
open fracture
chronic pain and diastasis in parturition-induced diastasis
or acute setng &gt;4-6cm
technique
for open fractures aggressive debridement according to
open fracture principles
anterior subcutaneous pelvic fixator (INFIX)
indications
same indications as anterior external fixation and
symphyseal plating
complications
heterotopic ossification, femoral nerve injury, infection
diverting colostomy
indications
consider in open pelvic fractures  |</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>especially with extensive perineal injury or rectal involvement</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="techniques">TECHNIQUES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#techniques" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Pelvic Binding</p>
<ul>
<li>otechnique</li>
<li>centered over greater trochanters to effect indirect reduction</li>
<li>do not place over iliac crest/abdomen</li>
<li>ineffective and precludes assessment of abdomen</li>
<li>may augment with internal rotation of lower extremities and taping at ankles</li>
<li>transition to alternative fixation as soon as possible</li>
<li>prolonged pressure from binder or sheet may cause skin necrosis</li>
<li>working portals may be cut in sheet to place percutaneous fixation</li>
<li>oearly pelvic binding and CT have been associated with underestimation of pelvic ring instability</li>
<li>fluroscopic exam under anesthesia can be used to assess stability in these circumstances</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rehabilitation">Rehabilitation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rehabilitation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>ostable fractures treated nonsurgical</li>
<li>patients may mobilize immediately with protected weight bearing after stable fracture pattern in confirmed (may require post-mobilization views to confirm stability)</li>
<li>ounstable fractures treated surgically</li>
<li>patient mobility and weight bearing depend on the location of the posterior pelvic ring fracture</li>
<li>mobility includes weight-of-limb weight bearing ipsilateral to the posterior pelvic injury with full weight bearing on contralateral side</li>
<li>patients with bilateral posterior pelvic ring injuries limited to bed-to-chair transfers only</li>
<li>when radiographic healing has occurred weight bearing can be gradually advanced</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Urogenital Injuries</li>
<li>present in 12-20% of patients with pelvic fractures</li>
<li>higher incidence in males (21%)</li>
<li>includes</li>
<li>posterior urethral tear</li>
<li>most common urogenital injury with pelvic ring fracture</li>
<li>bladder rupture</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>may see extravasation around the pubic symphysis
associated with mortality of 22-34%</p>
<p>diagnosis</p>
<ul>
<li>made with retrograde urethrocystogram</li>
<li>indications for retrograde urethrocystogram include</li>
<li>blood at meatus</li>
<li>high riding or excessively mobile prostate</li>
<li>hematuria</li>
</ul>
<p>treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>suprapubic catheter placement</li>
<li>suprapubic catheter is a relative contraindication to anterior ring plating</li>
<li>surgical repair</li>
<li>rupture should be repaired at the same time or prior to definitive fixation in order to minimize infection risk</li>
</ul>
<p>complications</p>
<ul>
<li>long-term complications common (up to 35%)</li>
<li>urethral stricture - most common</li>
<li>impotence</li>
<li>anterior pelvic ring infection</li>
<li>incontinence</li>
<li>parturition sequelae (i.e. caesarean section)</li>
</ul>
<p>Neurologic injury</p>
<ul>
<li>L5 nerve root runs over sacral ala joint</li>
<li>may be injured if SI screw is placed to anterior</li>
<li>anterior subcutaneous pelvic fixator may give rise to LFCN injury (most common) or femoral nerve injury</li>
</ul>
<p>oDVT and PE</p>
<ul>
<li>DVT in ~ 60%, PULMONARY EMBOLISM in ~ 27%, fatal PE in 2%</li>
<li>prophylaxis essential</li>
<li>mechanical compression</li>
<li>pharmacologic prevention (LMWH or Lovenox)</li>
<li>vena caval filters (closed head injury)</li>
</ul>
<p>oChronic instability</p>
<ul>
<li>rare complication; can be seen in nonoperative cases</li>
<li>presents with subjective instability and mechanical symptoms</li>
<li>diagnosed with alternating single-leg-stance pelvic radiographs (flamingo views)</li>
</ul>
<p>oInfection</p>
<ul>
<li>risk factors include:</li>
<li>obesity</li>
<li>diabetes</li>
</ul>
<p>85</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>prolonged operation time
prolonged ICU stay
larger amount of packed red blood cell transfusions,
associated genitourinary and abdominal trauma
open fractures
preoperative angioembolization is controversial</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fracture-acetabulum">FRACTURE ACETABULUM:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-acetabulum" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Acetabulum fractures are pelvis fractures that involve the articular
surface of the hip joint and may involve one or two columns, one or two walls, or the roof within the pelvis.
Diagnosis can be made radiographically with dedicated pelvis
radiographs (including Judet views) but frequently require CT pelvis for surgical planning.
Treatment can be nonoperative for non-displaced fractures but displaced injuries require anatomic open reduction and internal fixation to minimize development of post-traumatic osteoarthritis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="demographics">Demographics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#demographics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>ofractures occur in a bimodal distribution
high energy trauma in younger patients (e.g., motor vehicle accidents)
low energy trauma in elderly patients (e.g., fall from standing height)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>oPathoanatomy</p>
<p>fracture pattern predominately determined by
force vector
position of femoral head at time of injury
bone quality (e.g., age)</p>
<p>oAssociated conditions</p>
<p>orthopaedic manifestations
lower extremity injury (36%)
nerve palsy (13%)
most commonly seen in transverse + posterior wall
fracture patterns
most commonly affects the peroneal division of the
sciatic nerve
spine injury (4%)</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>systemic injuries</p>
<ul>
<li>head injury (19%)</li>
<li>chest injury (18%)</li>
<li>abdominal injury (8%)</li>
<li>genitourinary injury (6%)</li>
</ul>
