<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Nonoperative</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>hinged knee brace</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>indications (rare)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>stable, nondisplaced fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>nonambulatory patient</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>patient with significant comorbidities presenting an unacceptably high degree of surgical/anesthetic risk</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Non-operative treatment:</p>
<p>Continuous weight traction with limb supported on a Thomass splint with a knee flexion attachment.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>In displaced supracondylar fractures, the angle of knee flexion is very essential in reduction and stabilization of the fracture. Forward tilting of the distal fragment can be corrected by increasing the angle of knee flexion.</p>
<p>Radiographs should be taken to confirm reduction during the first few days after injury. These determine the correct position of the knee.</p>
<p>Avoid knee movements in the first 2 or 3 weeks in order not to disturb the position of the fragments.</p>
<p>Do active ankle, foot and toe exercises and static contractions of the quadriceps and gluteal muscles.</p>
<p>Full length plaster may be applied when the fracture shows signs of commencing union (sticky), often about 4-6 weeks after the injury.</p>
<p>This allows walking with partial weight bearing.</p>
<p>The plaster should be maintained for 6-8 weeks.</p>
<p>Operative reduction and internal fixation:</p>
<p>Has become the standard method of treatment in most centers.</p>
<p>It is the method of choice for the elderly to avoid prolonged recumbency.</p>
<p>Use of a combined nail-plate or a sliding screw and plate (Dynamic condylar screw) is recommended.</p>
<p>The nail or screw is driven horizontally across the lower fragment and the plate, at right angles to the nail, is screwed to the outer side of the main upper fragment.</p>
<p>Use of a long intramedullary nail driven down the femur and across the knee joint into the upper half of the tibia.</p>
<p>When the fracture is united, the nail is removed and knee movement is restored by active exercises.</p>
<p>Operative</p>
<p>external fixation</p>
<p>indications</p>
<p>temporizing measure to restore length, alignment, and stability</p>
<p>unstable, polytrauma</p>
<p>soft tissues not amenable to surgical incisions</p>
<p>and internal fixation, or until the patient is stable</p>
<p>contamination requiring multiple debridements</p>
<p>definitive treatment</p>
<p>severe open and/or comminuted fractures</p>
<p>118</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Mal-union</li>
<li>Stiffness of the knee</li>
<li>Injury to popliteal artery</li>
<li>Injury to a major nerve trunk (tibial nerve, common peroneal nerve).</li>
<li>Symptomatic hardware</li>
<li>Infection</li>
<li>Non union</li>
<li>Implant failure</li>
</ul>
<p>120</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fractures-of-femoral-condyle">FRACTURES OF FEMORAL CONDYLE:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures-of-femoral-condyle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Usually caused by direct violence to the region of the knee.</p>
<p>Two patterns of fracture are usually encountered:</p>
<ul>
<li>Oblique fracture shearing the condyle</li>
<li>T-shaped fracture separating both condyles</li>
</ul>
<p>The fracture may be a crack without displacement, or there may be complete separation of a condyle with marked displacement.</p>
<p>Depends on the degree of displacement.</p>
<p>Undisplaced fractures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Treated by immobilization in a long leg plaster for about 6-8 weeks, walking being allowed from an early stage.</li>
</ul>
<p>Displaced fractures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Accurate reduction is required</li>
<li>Reduction by traction and manipulation should be tried. If successful, continuous weight traction in Thomass splint can be done or use full length plaster.</li>
<li>If unsuccessful reduction, operative reduction and internal fixation should be done, e.g. by a long screw or bolt. T-shaped # can be treated by a right angled compression screw-plate (dynamic condylar screw)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Stiffness of the knee.</li>
<li>Caused by intra-articular adhesions and partly by peri-articular and intramuscular adhesions.</li>
<li>Treated by active exercises</li>
<li>Osteoarthritis of the knee</li>
<li>Injury to artery or nerve</li>
<li>Popliteal artery</li>
<li>One of the main nerve trunks</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="knee-dislocation">KNEE DISLOCATION:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#knee-dislocation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>121</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Devastating injury resulting from high or low energy high-energy</p>
<p>-usually from MVC or fall from height</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>commonly a dashboard injury resulting in axial load to flexed knee</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>low-energy</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>-often from athletic injury
-generally, has a rotational component
-morbid obesity is a risk-factor</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-b5eb9400.jpeg" alt="img-85.jpeg"></p>
<ul>
<li>About 1000000000 are high energy traumatic injuries characterized by a high rate of neurovascular injury.</li>
</ul>
<p>Diagnosis is made clinically with careful assessment of limb neurovascular status.</p>
<p>Radiographs should be obtained to document reduction.</p>
<p>Treatment is generally emergent reduction and stabilization with assessment of limb perfusion followed by delayed ligamentous reconstruction.</p>
<p>Associated injuries:</p>
<p>vascular injury</p>
<p>-5-15% in all dislocations
-40-50% in anterior/posterior dislocations
-due to tethering at the popliteal fossa</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>proximal - fibrous tunnel at the adductor hiatus</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>distal - fibrous tunnel at soleus muscle</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>nerve injury</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>-usually common peroneal nerve injury (25%)
-tibial nerve injury is less common</p>
<ul>
<li>fractures</li>
</ul>
<p>-present in 60%
-tibia and femur most common</p>
<p>CLASSIFICATION</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Descriptive</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Kennedy classification based on the direction of displacement of the tibia</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Kennedy classification</p>
<p>(based on the direction of displacement of the tibia)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>most common due to hyperextension injury</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Anterior (30-50%)</td><td>usually involves tear of PCL an arterial injury is generally an intimal tear due to traction the highest rate of peroneal nerve injury 2nd most common due to axial load to the flexed knee</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Posterior (30-40%)</td><td>(dashboard injury) the highest rate of vascular injury based on Kennedy classification has highest incidence of a complete tear of the popliteal artery</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Lateral (13%)</td><td>due to a varus or valgus force usually involves tears of both ACL and PCL</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Medial (3%)</td><td>varus or valgus force usually disrupted PLC and PCL usually irreducible</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Rotational (4%)</td><td>posterolateral is most common rotational dislocation buttonholing of femoral condyle through the capsule</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>based on a pattern of multiligamentous injury of knee dislocation (KD)</li>
</ul>
<p>Schenck Classification</p>
<p>(based on the number of ruptured ligaments)</p>
<p>Multiligamentous injury with the</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>KD I</th><th>involvement of the ACL or PCL Injury to ACL and PCL only (2</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>KD II</td><td>ligaments)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>KD III</td><td>Injury to ACL, PCL, and PMC or PLC (3</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>ligaments).</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Injury to ACL, PCL, PMC, and PLC (4 ligaments)</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>KD IV</td><td>Has the highest rate of vascular injury (5-15%)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>KD V</td><td>Multiligamentous injury with periarticular fracture</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>PRESENTATION</p>
<p>oSymptoms</p>
<ul>
<li>history of trauma and deformity of the knee</li>
<li>knee pain &amp; instability</li>
</ul>
<p>oPhysical exam</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>appearance</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>no obvious deformity</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>50% spontaneously reduce before arrival to ED</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>may present with subtle signs of trauma (swelling, effusion, abrasions, ecchymosis)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>obvious deformity</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>reduce immediately, especially if absent pulses</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>"dimple sign" - buttonholing of medial femoral condyle through the medial capsule</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>indicative of an irreducible posterolateral dislocation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>a contraindication to closed reduction due to risks of skin necrosis</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>vascular exam</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>priority is to rule out vascular injury on exam both before and after reduction</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>serial examinations are mandatory</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>palpate the dorsalis pedis and posterior tibial pulses on injured and contralateral side</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>if pulses are present and normal</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>does not indicate the absence of arterial injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>collateral circulation can mask a complete popliteal artery occlusion</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>measure Ankle-Brachial Index (ABI) on all patients with suspected KD</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>if ABI &gt;0.9</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>then monitor with serial examination (100% Negative Predictive Value)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>if ABI &lt;0.9</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>perform an arterial duplex ultrasound or CT angiography
if arterial injury confirmed then consult vascular surgery</p>
<p>If pulses are absent or diminished
confirm that the knee joint is reduced or perform immediate reduction and reassessment
immediate surgical exploration if pulses are still absent following reduction
ischemia time &gt;8 hours has amputation rates as high as 86%
imaging contraindicated if it will delay surgical revascularization
if pulses present after reduction then measure ABI then consider observation vs. angiography
neurologic exam
assess sensory and motor function of peroneal and tibial nerve as nerve deficits often occur concomitantly with vascular injuries
stability
diagnosis based on instability on physical exam (radiographs and gross appearance may be normal)
may see recurvatum when held in extension
assess ACL, PCL, MCL, LCL, and PLC</p>
<p>TREATMENT</p>
<p>oNonoperative</p>
<ul>
<li>emergent closed reduction followed by vascular assessment/consult indications</li>
<li>considered an orthopedic emergency</li>
<li>vascular consult indicated if</li>
<li>pulses are absent or diminished following reduction</li>
<li>if arterial injury confirmed by arterial duplex ultrasound or CT angiography</li>
<li>immobilization as definitive management</li>
<li>indications (rare)</li>
<li>successful closed reduction without vascular compromise</li>
<li>most cases require some form of surgical stabilization following reduction</li>
<li>outcomes</li>
<li>worse outcomes are seen with nonoperative management</li>
<li>prolonged immobilization will lead to loss of ROM with persistent instability</li>
</ul>
<p>oOperative</p>
<ul>
<li>open reduction</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>irreducible knee</li>
<li>posterolateral dislocation</li>
<li>open fracture-dislocation</li>
<li>obesity (may be difficult to obtain closed)</li>
<li>vascular injury</li>
<li>external fixation</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>indications</p>
<ul>
<li>vascular repair (takes precedence)</li>
<li>open fracture-dislocation</li>
<li>compartment syndrome</li>
<li>obese (if difficult to maintain reduction)</li>
<li>polytrauma patient</li>
<li>delayed ligamentous reconstruction/repair</li>
</ul>
<p>indications</p>
<ul>
<li>instability will require some kind of ligamentous repair or fixation</li>
<li>patients can be placed in a knee immobilizer until treated operatively</li>
<li>improved outcomes with early treatment (within 3 weeks)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="technique">TECHNIQUE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#technique" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Closed reduction</p>
<ul>
<li>approach</li>
<li>anterior dislocation - traction and anterior translation of the femur</li>
<li>posterior dislocation - traction, extension, and anterior translation of the tibia</li>
<li>medial/lateral - traction and medial or lateral translation</li>
<li>rotatory - axial limb traction and rotation in the opposite direction of deformity</li>
<li>osplinting</li>
<li>20 to 30 degrees of flexion</li>
</ul>
<p>Complications:</p>
<ul>
<li>Stiffness (arthrofibrosis)</li>
<li>is most common complication</li>
<li>more common with delayed mobilization</li>
<li>Laxity and instability</li>
<li>Peroneal nerve injury</li>
<li>most common in posterolateral dislocations</li>
<li>poor results with acute, subacute, and delayed (&gt;3 months) nerve exploration</li>
<li>neurolysis and tendon transfers are the mainstay of treatment</li>
<li>Vascular compromise</li>
<li>in addition to vessel damage, claudication, skin changes, and muscle atrophy can occur</li>
</ul>
<p>126</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tibial-plateau-fracture">TIBIAL PLATEAU FRACTURE:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tibial-plateau-fracture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>MECHANISM OF INJURY.</p>
<p>Tibial Plateau fractures are periarticular injuries of the proximal tibia frequently associated with soft tissue injury.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made with knee radiographs but frequently require CT scan for surgical planning.</p>
<p>Treatment is often ORIF in the acute setting versus delayed fixation after soft tissue swelling subsides.</p>
<p>127</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>This is sometimes the result of a car striking a pedestrian (bumper fracture) more often it is due to a fall from a height in which the knee is forced into valgus or Varus.</p>
<p>The tibia condyle is crushed or split by the opposing femoral condyle which remains intact.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-740c4e67.jpeg" alt="img-86.jpeg">
PATHOLOGICAL ANATOMY.</p>
<p>Location</p>
<p>olateral plateau 70-80%</p>
<p>obicondylar 10-30%</p>
<p>omedial plateau 10-20%</p>
<p>The fracture pattern and degree of displacement depend on the type of direction of force as well as the quality of the bone at the upper end of tibia.</p>
<p>Associated conditions</p>
<p>omeniscal tears</p>
<p>lateral meniscal tear</p>
<p>more common than medial</p>
<p>associated with Schatzker II fracture pattern</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>associated with  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex-error" title="ParseError: KaTeX parse error: Expected 'EOF', got '&amp;' at position 1: &amp;gt;10\mathrm{mm" style="color: rgb(204, 0, 0);">&amp;gt;10\mathrm{mm}</span></span>  articular depression</p>
<p>associated with  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex-error" title="ParseError: KaTeX parse error: Expected 'EOF', got '&amp;' at position 1: &amp;gt;6\mathrm{mm}" style="color: rgb(204, 0, 0);">&amp;gt;6\mathrm{mm}</span></span>  condylar widening</p>
<p>medial meniscal tear</p>
<p>most commonly associated with Schatzker IV fractures</p>
<p>oACL injuries</p>
<p>more common in type IV and VI fractures (25%)</p>
<p>o compartment syndrome</p>
<p>oassociated soft tissue injuries have little bearing on final outcomes</p>
<p>oneurovascular injury</p>
<p>commonly associated with Schatzker IV fracture-dislocations</p>
<p>common peroneal nerve is most common nerve injury</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hohl-and-moore-classification">Hohl and Moore Classification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hohl-and-moore-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>OUseful for</p>
<p>true fracture-dislocations</p>
<p>fracture patterns that do not fit into the Schatzker classification (10% of all tibial plateau fractures)</p>
<p>fractures associated with knee instability</p>
<p>oHohl and Moore Classification of proximal tibia fracture-dislocations</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oType I</th><th>oCoronal split fracture</th><th>o</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oType</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>II oEntire condylar fracture</td><td>o</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>III oRim avulsion fracture of lateral plateau</td><td>o</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>IV oRim compression fracture</td><td>o</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>VoFour-part fracture</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Schatzker classification</p>
<p>oSchatzker Classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oType I</th><th>oLateral split fracture</th><th>oyoung patient with strong subchondral</th><th>o</th></tr></thead></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>bone</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oLateral Split-</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oType II</td><td>depressed fracture</td><td>omost common</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oLateral Pure depression</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType III</td><td>fracture oDepression of weight bearing part leaving the condylar rim intact</td><td>ouncommon, elderly osteoporotic oassociated fx-</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td>oType IV</td><td>oMedial plateau fracture</td><td>dislocation ohigh rate of NV and ligamentous injuries o otibial spines remain</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType V</td><td>oBicondylar fracture</td><td>continuous with shaft</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oMetaphyseal-diaphyseal</td><td>osignificant soft-tissue</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType VI</td><td>disassociation</td><td>injury</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-features">CLINICAL FEATURES.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-features" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>The knee is swollen</li>
<li>Deformity</li>
<li>Bruises</li>
<li>Pain</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="odifferential">ODIFFERENTIAL<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#odifferential" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Distal femur fracture</li>
<li>Knee dislocation</li>
<li>Patella instability</li>
<li>Patella fracture</li>
<li>Patella tendon rupture</li>
<li>Quadriceps tendon rupture</li>
<li>ACL tear</li>
<li>Meniscus tear</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Radiographs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>recommended views</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>AP</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>lateral</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>oblique</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>oblique is helpful to determine amount of depression</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>findings</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>on AP</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>depressed articular surface</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>sclerotic band of bone indicating depression</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>abnormal joint alignment</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>on lateral</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>posteromedial fracture lines must be recognized</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>abnormal tibial slope</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>CT scan</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>indication</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>negative radiographs with high index of suspicion for tibial plateau</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>preoperative planning</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>obtain after ex-fix if definitive fixation delayed if soft-tissues are not amenable for surgery</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>findings</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>articular depression</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>degree of comminution</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>fracture plane and location</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>posterior coronal split fracture best appreciated on axial and sagittal views</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>lipohemarthrosis indicates an occult fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>MRI</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>indications</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>identify meniscal and ligamentous pathology</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>occult fractures</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>131</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Nonoperative</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>oclosed reduction / immobilization</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>minimally displaced split or depressed fractures</li>
<li>low energy fracture stable to varus/valgus alignment</li>
<li>nonambulatory patients</li>
<li>significant comorbidities that preclude surgical intervention</li>
<li>modalities</li>
<li>patella-tendon-bearing (PTB) cast A/K cast</li>
<li>knee immobilizer</li>
<li>hinged knee brace</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Operative</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>oORIF</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>articular depression &gt; 5-10 mm</li>
<li>condylar widening &gt; 5mm</li>
<li>varus/valgus instability &gt;10 deg</li>
<li>medial plateau fractures</li>
<li>bicondylar fractures</li>
</ul>
<p>postoperative infection after ORIF associated with</p>
<ul>
<li>male gender</li>
<li>smoking</li>
<li>pulmonary disease</li>
<li>bicondylar fracture patterns</li>
<li>intraoperative time over 3 hours</li>
<li>ligamentous instability</li>
</ul>
<p>external fixation/Ilizarov +/- limited open/percutaneous fixation of articular segment</p>
<ul>
<li>indications</li>
<li>severe open fracture with marked contamination</li>
<li>highly comminuted fractures where internal fixation not possible</li>
</ul>
<p>arthroplasty</p>
<ul>
<li>indications</li>
<li>consider in patients &gt;65-years-old with osteoporotic bone</li>
</ul>
<p>132</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>outcomes</p>
<p>earlier time to weight bearing
improved outcomes for primary</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="principles-in-reduction-and-fixation">PRINCIPLES IN REDUCTION AND FIXATION.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principles-in-reduction-and-fixation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Traction is used to achieve reduction many of the fragments that have soft tissue attachments will reduce spontaneously (Ligamentotaxis).</p>
<p>If open reduction is needed or intended, the operation should be carefully planned.</p>
<p>Stability is all important, no matter which method is used, fixation must be secure enough to permit early joint movement. There is little point in ending up with a pleasing x-ray and a stiff knee.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Post-traumatic arthritis</strong>
3-7% undergo TKA at 10+ years</p>
<ul>
<li>risk factors for arthritis</li>
<li>meniscectomy</li>
<li>malalignment &gt; 5 deg</li>
<li>instability</li>
<li>risk factors for future TKA</li>
<li>age</li>
<li>bicondylar fracture</li>
<li>increasing comorbidities</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Compartment syndrome</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>Schatzker type IV</li>
<li>high-energy mechanism</li>
<li>associated fibula fracture</li>
<li>fracture length</li>
<li>associated plateau-shaft injury</li>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>emergent fasciotomy</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Infection</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>poor surgical timing based on swelling</li>
</ul>
<p>133</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>open fractures</p>
<p>longer operative time</p>
<p>treatment</p>
<p>irrigation and debridement + IV abx</p>
<p>removal of hardware if loose or grossly infected</p>
<p>ex-fix and staged revision ORIF</p>
<p>retain hardware if fracture still healing and implant still</p>
<p>providing stability</p>
<p>oNonunion/malunion</p>
<p>ouncommon due to rich blood supply of cancellous bone</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>Schatzker type VI (metaphyseal-diaphyseal junction)</p>
<p>comminution</p>
<p>unstable fixation</p>
<p>treatment</p>
<p>revision osteosynthesis augmented with bone graft</p>
<p>oKnee stiffness</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>increasing age</p>
<p>higher BMI</p>
<p>severity of fracture</p>
<p>prolonged immobilization</p>
<p>involvement of tibial eminence</p>
<p>polytrauma</p>
<p>treatment</p>
<p>arthroscopic lysis of adhesions</p>
<p>indicated if unable to achieve 90 deg of flexion within 4</p>
<p>weeks</p>
<p>oLoss of reduction</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>inadequate fixation</p>
<p>severity of fracture</p>
<p>osteoporosis</p>
<p>treatment</p>
<p>revision ORIF to address inadequate fixation</p>
<p>Deep vein thrombosis</p>
<p>incidence</p>
<p>nonoperative 9%</p>
<p>operative 6%</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="fractture-shaft-tibia">FRACTTURE SHAFT TIBIA:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractture-shaft-tibia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Diaphyseal tibial fractures are the most common long bone fracture.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is confirmed by plain radiographs of the tibia and adjacent joints.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Treatment is generally operative with intramedullary nailing. In rare cases, external fixation or ORIF is more appropriate depending on the location and orientation of the fracture</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Pathophysiology:</strong></p>
<p><strong>mechanism of injury</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>low energy (fall from standing, twisting, etc)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>result of indirect, torsional injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>leads to spiral fracture pattern with fibula fracture at a different level</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>more likely to be associated with a lower degree of soft tissue injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>high energy fx (MVA, fall from height, athletics, etc)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>result of direct force</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>leads to wedge or short oblique fracture that may have significant comminution with fibula fracture at same level</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>more likely to be associated with severe soft tissue injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Oestern and Tscherne II / III</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>open fractures</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>pathoanatomy</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>proximal third tibia fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>must rule out extension into tibial plateau on plain films or CT scan</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>high risk for valgus/procurvatum deformity with IM nailing</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>distal third tibia fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>higher rates of ankle injury seen with distal 1/3 tibia fracture and spiral fracture pattern</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>posterior malleolus most common associated ankle injury which, in some cases, may affect syndesmotic stability</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>extension into or adjacent to tibial plafond may require separate/additional fixation and are managed differently than tibial shaft fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>associated conditions</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>soft tissue injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>severity of muscle injury has highest impact on eventual need for amputation</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>compartment syndrome</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>more common in diaphyseal tibial shaft fractures than proximal or distal tibia fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>8.1% risk in diaphyseal fractures, compared to proximal (1.6%) and distal (1.4%) fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>can occur even in the setting of an open fracture</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>all four compartments must be examined. If patient is unable to participate in examination and concern is high clinically, intracompartmental compartment measurements should be performed</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>bone loss</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>ipsilateral skeletal injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>tibial plateau fractures</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>135</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>tibial plafond fractures</p>
<p>femoral shaft fractures</p>
<p>floating knee is an indication for antegrade tibial nailing and retrograde femoral nailing</p>
<p>posterior malleolar fracture</p>
<p>distal 1/3 and spiral tibial shaft fractures</p>
<p>CLASSIFICATION</p>
<p>Fracture classification is primarily descriptive based on pattern and location</p>
<p>oOTA Classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>o42A oSimple fracture patterns</th><th>o</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>o42B oWedge patterns</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td>o42C oComplex/comminuted patterns</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>oOestern and Tscherne Classification of Closed Fracture Soft Tissue Injury</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oGrade 0</th><th>oInjuries from indirect forces with negligible soft-tissue damage</th><th>o</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oGrade I</td><td>oSuperficial contusion/abrasion, simple fractures o</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oGrade II</td><td>oDeep abrasions, muscle/skin contusion, direct trauma, impending compartment syndrome o</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oGrade III</td><td>oExcessive skin contusion, crushed skin or destruction of muscle, subcutaneous degloving, acute compartment syndrome, and rupture of major blood vessel or nerve o o</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>oGustilo-Anderson Classification of Open Tibia Fractures</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>oLimited periosteal stripping, clean wound &lt;</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oType I</td><td>1 cm</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oMinimal periosteal stripping, wound &gt;1 cm</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType II</td><td>in length without extensive soft-tissue injury damage</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oSignificant soft tissue injury (often evidenced by a segmental fracture or comminution), significant periosteal stripping, wound usually &gt;5cm in length, no flap required.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType IIIA</td><td></td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td>oType</td><td>oSignificant periosteal stripping and soft tissue injury, flap required due to inadequate soft tissue coverage . Treat proximal 1/3 fxs with gastrocnemius</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td>IIIB</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>o</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oType IIIC</td><td>oSignificant soft tissue injury (often evidenced by a segmental fracture or comminution), vascular injury requiring repair to maintain limb viability</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oFor prognostic reasons, severely comminuted, contaminated barnyard injuries, close-range shotgun/high-velocity gunshot injuries, and open fractures presenting over 24 hours from injury have all been included in the grade III group.</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>o</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Radiographs</p>
<p>orecommended views</p>
<p>full-length AP and lateral views of the affected tibia</p>
<p>AP, lateral and oblique views of ipsilateral knee and ankle</p>
<p>repeat radiographs recommended after splinting or fracture manipulation</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nonoperative</p>
<p>closed reduction / cast immobilization</p>
<p>indications</p>
<p>closed, low energy fractures with acceptable alignment</p>
<p>&lt; 5 degrees varus-valgus angulation
&lt; 10 degrees anterior/posterior angulation
&gt; 50% cortical apposition
&lt; 1 cm shortening
&lt; 10 degrees rotational malalignment</p>
<p>certain patients who may be non-ambulatory (ie. paralyzed), or those unfit for surgery</p>
<p>Operative</p>
<p>I&amp;D + antibiotics</p>
<p>indications</p>
<p>all open tibia fractures require an emergent I&amp;D</p>
<p>surgical debridement within 12-24 hours of injury</p>
<p>wounds should be irrigated and dressed with saline-soaked gauze in the</p>
<p>emergency department before splinting</p>
<p>all open tibia fractures require immediate antibiotics</p>
<p>should be administered within 3 hours of injury</p>
<p>standard abx for open fractures (institution dependent)</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>cephalosporin given continuously for 24 hours after definitive surgery in Grade I, II, and IIIA open fractures</p>
<p>aminoglycoside added in Grade IIIB injuries
penicillin administered in farm injuries theoretically covers Clostridium
tetanus vaccination status should be confirmed and appropriate prophylaxis should be administered if necessary</p>
<p>external fixation
indications
damage control for polytrauma patients
open fractures with soft tissue defects/contamination
proximal or distal metaphyseal fractures</p>
<p>IM Nailing
indications
unacceptable alignment with closed reduction and casting
soft tissue injury that will not tolerate casting
segmental fx
severely comminuted fx
ipsilateral limb injury (i.e., floating knee)
polytrauma
bilateral tibia fx
morbid obesity
risks for nonunion:
gapping at fracture site,
open fracture
transverse fracture pattern
shorter immobilization time,
earlier time to weight-bearing,
decreased time to union compared to casting</p>
<p>amputation
indications
no current scoring system to determine if an amputation should be performed
relative indications for amputation include
significant soft tissue trauma
warm ischemia &gt; 6 hrs
severe ipsilateral foot trauma</p>
<p>COMPLICATIONS
oAnterior knee pain
oMalunion
incidence
all tibial shaft fractures - between 8-10%</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>higher in proximal 1/3 tibia fractures - up to 50%</p>
<p>valgus/procurvatum deformity</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>definitive management with casting or external fixation</p>
<p>most common deformity is varus with nonsurgical</p>
<p>management</p>
<p>varus malunion may place patient at risk for</p>
<p>ipsilateral ankle pain and stiffness</p>
<p>oNonunion (no healing at 9 months)</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>open fracture</p>
<p>cortical contact &lt;50%</p>
<p>transverse fracture pattern</p>
<p>oMalrotation</p>
<p>incidence</p>
<p>highest after IM nailing of distal 1/3 tibia fractures</p>
<p>increases risk of adjacent ankle arthrosis</p>
<p>oCompartment syndrome</p>
<p>incidence</p>
<p>estimated between 1-9%</p>
<p>can occur in both closed and open tibia shaft fractures</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>high energy injuries</p>
<p>significant soft tissue injuries</p>
<p>treatment</p>
<p>emergent four-compartment fasciotomy</p>
<p>oNerve injury</p>
<p>oInfection</p>
<p>incidence</p>
<p>approximately 5%</p>
<p>risk factors</p>
<p>open fracture</p>
<p>severe soft tissue injury with contamination</p>
<p>longer time to definitive soft tissue coverage</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-a8c67a63.jpeg" alt="img-87.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tibial-plafond-fractures">TIBIAL PLAFOND FRACTURES:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tibial-plafond-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A tibial plafond fracture (also known as a pilon fracture) is a fracture of the distal end of the tibia, most commonly associated with comminution, intra-articular extension, and significant soft tissue injury.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is typically made through clinical evaluation and confirmed with plain radiographs.</p>
<p>Treatment is generally operative with temporary external fixation followed by delayed open reduction internal fixation once the soft tissues permit.</p>
<p>140</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-8ed26615.jpeg" alt="img-88.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Pathophysiology mechanism</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>high energy axial load (most common)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>talus is driven into the plafond resulting in articular impaction of the distal tibia</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>falls from height</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>motor vehicle accidents</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>low energy rotational forces (less common)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Associated conditions</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>75% have associated fibula fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>30% have an ipsilateral lower extremity injury</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>20% are open fractures</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>5-10% are bilateral pilon fractures</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>0</p>
<p>AO/OTA Classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>43-A</th><th>Extra-articular</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>43-B</td><td>Partial articular</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>43-C</td><td>Complete articular</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Ruedi and Allgower Classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type I</th><th>Nondisplaced</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Type II</td><td>Simple displacement with incongruous joint</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Type III</td><td>Comminuted articular surface</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="presentation">PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Symptoms</p>
<ul>
<li>severe ankle pain</li>
<li>ankle deformity</li>
<li>inability to bear weight</li>
</ul>
<p>Physical exam</p>
<ul>
<li>inspection &amp; palpation</li>
<li>ankle tenderness, swelling, abrasions, ecchymosis, fracture blisters, open wounds, and chronic skin/vascular changes</li>
<li>motion</li>
<li>ankle motion limited</li>
<li>neurovascular</li>
<li>check DP and PT pulses</li>
<li>CT angiography if clinically warranted</li>
<li>look for neurologic compromise</li>
<li>check for signs/symptoms of compartment syndrome</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Radiographs</p>
<ul>
<li>recommended views</li>
<li>AP</li>
<li>lateral</li>
<li>mortise</li>
<li>full-length tibia/fibula and foot x-rays performed for fracture extension</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nonoperative</p>
<ul>
<li>cast immobilization</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>stable fracture patterns without articular surface displacement</li>
<li>critically ill or non-ambulatory patients</li>
<li>significant risk of skin problems (diabetes, vascular disease, peripheral neuropathy)</li>
<li>open reduction and internal fixation (ORIF)</li>
<li>indications</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<ul>
<li>definitive fixation for a majority of pilon fractures</li>
<li>limited or definitive ORIF can be performed acutely with low complications in certain situations</li>
<li>external fixation/circular frame fixation alone</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>oselect cases where bone or soft tissue injury precludes internal fixation</li>
<li>primary ankle arthrodesis</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>ono definitive indications</li>
<li>potential indications</li>
<li>oseverely comminuted, non-reconstructable plafond fractures</li>
<li>oselect elderly populations who cannot tolerate multiple surgeries or prolonged immobilization</li>
<li>omanual laborers</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Infection</li>
<li>incidence 5-15%</li>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>significant soft tissue swelling at time of definitive surgery</li>
<li>treatment</li>
<li>irrigation and debridement, antibiotics, possible hardware removal</li>
<li>oMalunion</li>
<li>oNonunion</li>
<li>risk factors</li>
<li>metaphyseal comminution</li>
<li>open fractures</li>
<li>bone loss</li>
<li>tobacco use/NSAID use</li>
<li>oPost-traumatic arthritis</li>
<li>oStiffness</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-tarsal-bone">Fracture tarsal bone<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-tarsal-bone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-navicular">I: NAVICULAR:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-navicular" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Tarsal Navicular Fractures are rare fractures of the midfoot that may occur due to trauma or due to repetitive micro stress.</li>
<li>Diagnosis can be made with plain radiographs of the foot.</li>
<li>Treatment is generally nonoperative with cast immobilization and non-weight-bearing for the majority of fractures. Surgical management is indicated for nonunion, significantly displaced fractures, for elite athletes.</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Navicular fractures can be traumatic stress fracture</p>
<p>mechanism of injury is usually due to chronic overuse</p>
<ul>
<li>often seen in athletes running on hard surfaces</li>
<li>also common in baseball players</li>
<li>considered a high-risk injury due to risk of AVN</li>
<li>most common complications include delayed union and non-union</li>
</ul>
<p>Spontaneous navicular AVN (Mueller-Weiss syndrome)</p>
<p>Spontaneous navicular AVN is a rare disease that and can be seen in middle aged adults with chronic midfoot pain</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="anatomy">ANATOMY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anatomy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Articulations</p>
<ul>
<li>onavicular bone articulates with</li>
<li>cuneiforms</li>
<li>cuboid</li>
<li>calcaneus</li>
<li>talus</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>|  oSangeorzan Classification of Navicular Body Fractures</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>(based on plane of fracture and degree of comminution)</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>oTransverse fracture of dorsal fragment that involves &lt; 50% of bone.</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType I</td><td>oNo associated deformity</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oOblique fracture, usually from dorsal-lateral to plantar-medial.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType II</td><td>oMay have forefoot ADduction deformity.</td><td>o</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>oCentral or lateral comminution.</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>oType III</td><td>oABduction deformity.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="presentation">PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Symptoms</li>
<li>midfoot pain and swelling</li>
<li>Physical exam</li>
<li>midfoot swelling</li>
<li>tenderness to palpation of midfoot</li>
<li>usually full ROM of ankle and subtalar joint</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Radiographs</li>
<li>may be difficult to see and are often missed</li>
<li>recommended views</li>
<li>AP</li>
<li>lateral</li>
<li>oblique 45 degree radiograph</li>
<li>best to visualize tuberosity fractures</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-stress-fractures">TREATMENT OF STRESS FRACTURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-stress-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Nonoperative</li>
<li>cast immobilization with no weight bearing</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>any navicular stress fracture, regardless of type, can be initially treated with cast immobilization and nonweight bearing for 6-8 weeks with high rates of success</li>
<li>Operative</li>
<li>open reduction and internal fixation</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>high level athletes</li>
<li>nonunion of navicular stress fracture</li>
<li>failure of cast immobilization and non weight bearing</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment-of-traumatic-fractures">TREATMENT OF TRAUMATIC FRACTURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment-of-traumatic-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Nonoperative</li>
<li>cast immobilization with no weight bearing</li>
<li>indications</li>
<li>acute avulsion fractures</li>
<li>most tuberosity fractures</li>
<li>minimally displaced Type I and II navicular body fractures</li>
<li>Operative</li>
<li>fragment excision</li>
<li>indications</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>avulsion fractures that failed to improve with nonoperative modalities
tuberosity fractures that went on to symptomatic nonunion
open reduction and internal fixation
indications
avulsion fractures involving &gt; 25% of articular surface
tuberosity fractures with &gt; 5mm diastasis or large intra-articular fragment
displaced or intra-articular Type I and II navicular body fractures</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fracture-calcaneus">Fracture calcaneus:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fracture-calcaneus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Calcaneus fractures are the most common fractured tarsal bone and are associated with a high degree of morbidity and disability.
Diagnosis is made radiographically with foot radiographs with CT scan often being required for surgical planning.
Treatment is nonoperative versus operative based on fracture displacement and alignment, associated soft tissue injury, and patient risk factors.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>oExtra-articular (25%)</p>
<p>oIntra-articular (75%)</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Sanders classification</p>
<p>based on the number of articular fragments seen on the coronal CT image at the widest point of the posterior facet</p>
<p>Sanders classification</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type</th><th>Nondisplaced posterior facet (regardless of number of fracture lines)</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Type II</td><td>One fracture line in the posterior facet (two fragments)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Type III</td><td>Two fracture lines in the posterior facet (three fragments)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Type IV</td><td>Comminuted with more than three fracture lines in the posterior facet (four or more fragments)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Beavis classification</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>based on fracture morphology of the calcaneus tuberosity</p>
<p>Beavis Classification</p>
<p>(based on fracture of tuberosity)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type 1</th><th>Sleeve fracture - small shell of cortical bone avulses from the tuberosity</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Type 2</td><td>Beak fracture - oblique fracture line runs posteriorly from most superior portion of the posterior facet</td></tr><tr><td>Type 3</td><td>Infrabursal fracture from the middle of the tuberosity</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="presentation">PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>oSymptoms</p>
<p>pain</p>
<p>swelling</p>
<p>inability to bear weight</p>
<p>gross deformity</p>
<p>open fracture</p>
<p>oPhysical exam</p>
<p>inspection</p>
<p>ecchymosis and swelling</p>
<p>shortened and widened heel</p>
<p>may have apparent varus deformity</p>
<p>open skin lesions or fractures</p>
<p>posterior heel skin compromise</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>fracture blisters
palpation
diffuse tenderness to palpation
strength
decreased ankle plantarflexion strength with avulsion fractures
neurologic
assess for neurologic compromise due to swelling
vascular
assess peripheral pulses</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Radiographs
orecommended views
AP
lateral
oblique</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nonoperative
ocast immobilization with non weight bearing for 6 weeks
indications
calcaneal stress fractures
ocast immobilization with non-weight bearing for 10 to 12 weeks
indications
small extra-articular fracture (&lt;1 cm) with intact Achilles tendon and &lt;2 mm displacement
Sanders Type I (nondisplaced)
anterior process fracture involving &lt;25% of calcaneocuboid joint comorbidities
minimally displaced tuberosity fractures (&lt;1 cm of displacement) without threatened soft-tissue envelope in elderly patients with reduced function or physical capacity</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="complications">COMPLICATIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Wound complications (10-25%)
odelayed wound healing is the most common complication</p>
<p>Subtalar arthritis</p>
<p>Compartment syndrome (10%)
oresults in claw toes</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Malunion</p>
<p>0</p>
<p>Malunion CT Classification &amp; Treatment</p>
<p>Lateral exostosis with no subtalar</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type</th><th>arthritis</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>I</td><td>Treat with lateral wall resection</td></tr><tr><td>Type</td><td>Lateral exostosis with subtalar arthritis</td></tr><tr><td>II</td><td>Treat with lateral wall resection and subtalar fusion</td></tr><tr><td>Type</td><td>Lateral exostosis, subtalar arthritis, and varus malunion</td></tr><tr><td>III</td><td>Treat with lateral wall resection, subtalar fusion, and +/- valgus osteotomy (controversial)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>FRACTURE METATARSALS:</p>
<p>Metatarsal fractures are among the most common injuries of the foot that may occur due to trauma or repetitive microstress.</p>
<p>Diagnosis is made with plain radiographs of the foot.</p>
<p>Treatment may be nonoperative or operative depending on the specific metatarsal involved, number of metatarsals involved, and fracture displacement.</p>
<p>ETIOLOGY</p>
<p>Mechanism</p>
<p>direct crush injury</p>
<p>may have significant associated soft tissue injury</p>
<p>indirect mechanism (most common)</p>
<p>149</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>occurs with forefoot fixed and hindfoot or leg rotating
oAssociated conditions
Lisfranc injury
Lisfranc equivalent injuries seen with multiple proximal
metatarsal fractures
stress fracture
consider metabolic evaluation for fragility fracture
look for associated foot deformity
seen at base of 2nd metatarsal in ballet dancers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>oClassification of metatarsal fractures is descriptive and should include
location
fracture pattern
displacement
angulation
articular involvement</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="presentation">PRESENTATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>oHistory
look for antecedent pain when suspicious for stress fracture
oSymptoms
pain, inability to bear weight
oPhysical Exam
inspection
foot alignment (neutral, cavovarus, planovalgus)
focal areas or diffuse areas of tenderness
careful soft tissue evaluation with crush or high-energy injuries
motion
evaluate for overlapping or malrotation with motion
neurovascular
semmes weinstein monofilament testing if suspicious for
peripheral neuropathy</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="imaging">IMAGING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#imaging" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Radiographs
orecommended views
required
AP, lateral and oblique views of the foot</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">TREATMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Nonoperative
ostiff soled shoe or walking boot with weight bearing as tolerated
indications</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>first metatarsal
non-displaced fractures
second through fourth (central) metatarsals
isolated fractures
non-displaced or minimally displaced fractures
stress fractures
second metatarsal most common
look for metabolic bone disease
evaluate for cavovarus foot with recurrent stress
fractures</p>
<p>Operative
opercutaneous vs open reduction and fixation
indications
open fractures
first metatarsal
any displacement
no intermetatarsal ligament support
30-50% of weight bearing with gait</p>
<p>COMPLICATIONS
oMalunion
may lead to transfer metatarsalgia or plantar keratosis
treat with osteotomy to correct deformity</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="open-fracture-management">OPEN FRACTURE MANAGEMENT:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#open-fracture-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-2dcf6884.jpeg" alt="img-89.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-e6a61a62.jpeg" alt="img-90.jpeg"></p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Open fractures are fractures with direct communication to the external environment. Diagnosis is made clinically by assessing the size and nature of the external wound as well as obtaining radiographs of the bone at the location of the soft tissue injury. Treatment depends on location of fracture but generally requires immediate IV antibiotics and urgent irrigation and debridement followed by surgical fixation as needed.</p>
<p>Definition:</p>
<p>A fracture is open or compound when there is a wound of the skin surface leading down to the site of fracture.</p>
<p>A fracture is classed as open only when a direct communication exists between the body surface and the fractured bone ends.</p>
<p>TREATMENT</p>
<p>Do primary survey  ABC</p>
<p>AIRWAY</p>
<p>Ensure airway is clear</p>
<p>BREATHING</p>
<p>Make sure the patient is ventilating and if not assist (Ambu bag, oxygen)</p>
<p>CIRCULATION</p>
<p>Assess for bleeding  inspect, pulse, BP</p>
<p>Start an IV line</p>
<p>Give IV fluids to restore volume</p>
<p>Stop obvious bleeding</p>
<p>Blood for grouping and cross-match, Hb, haematocrit, blood gases.</p>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260204-26b7d1ae.jpeg" alt="img-91.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="etiology">ETIOLOGY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Pathophysiology</li>
<li>mechanism of injury</li>
<li>high-energy trauma</li>
<li>"inside-out" open fractures</li>
<li>Associated conditions</li>
<li>often associated with additional injuries (30%)</li>
<li>compartment syndrome</li>
<li>the presence of an open wound does not preclude the occurrence of compartment syndrome in the injured limb</li>
</ul>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Gustilo classification</p>
<p>Tscherne classification</p>
<p>Gustilo Classification
I II IIIA IIIB IIIC</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Energy Low</th><th>Moderate</th><th>High</th><th>High</th><th>High</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>usually</td><td>usually</td><td>usually</td></tr><tr><td>Wound size</td><td>1 cm</td><td></td><td>1-10 cm</td><td>&gt;10 cm</td><td></td><td>&gt;10 cm</td><td>&gt;10 cm</td></tr><tr><td>Soft tissue damage</td><td>Minimal</td><td>Moderate</td><td></td><td>Extensive</td><td>Extensive</td><td>Extensive</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Contamination Clean</td><td>Moderate</td><td></td><td></td><td>Extensive</td><td>Extensive</td><td>Extensive</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Fracture Comminution</td><td>Minimal</td><td>Moderate</td><td>Severe</td><td>Severe</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Periosteal Stripping</td><td>No</td><td>No</td><td>Yes</td><td>Yes</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>Free tissue</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Skin Coverage</td><td>Local coverage</td><td></td><td>Local coverage</td><td>Local coverage</td><td>Flax coverage</td><td>Flap or rotational flap coverage</td><td>Typically requires flap coverage</td></tr><tr><td>Neurovascular Injury</td><td>Normal</td><td>Normal</td><td>Normal</td><td>Normal</td><td></td><td></td><td>Exposed fracture with arterial damage that requires repair</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>TRAUMATOLOGY TWO. (COTM)</p>
<p>Antibiotics based on Gustilo Classification</p>
